ol.enhanced-list ol.enhanced-list .list-item {
    counter-increment: list-counter-alpha;
}

ol.enhanced-list ol.enhanced-list .list-marker::before {
    content: counter(list-counter-alpha, lower-alpha);
}

/* Level 3: Lowercase roman numerals */

    counter-reset: list-counter-roman;
}

    counter-increment: list-counter-roman;
}

ol.enhanced-list ol.enhanced-list ol.enhanced-list .list-marker::before {
    content: counter(list-counter-roman, lower-roman);
}

<p><strong>Disaster:</strong> The World Health Organization (WHO) defines a disaster as a sudden ecological phenomenon of sufficient magnitude to require external assistance.</p>
<p>It is also defined as any event, typically occurring suddenly, that causes damage, ecological disruption, loss of human life, deterioration of health and health services, and which exceeds the capacity of the affected community on a scale sufficient to require outside assistance (Landsman, 2001).</p>
<p>It is an emergency of such severity and magnitude that the resultant combination of deaths, injuries, illness, and property damage cannot be effectively managed with routine procedures or resources.</p>
<p>Disaster is further defined as an event in which a society or a community undergoes acute deprivation of food and other basic necessities due to natural and man-made calamities to such an extent that the normal function of the society or the community is disrupted and that it cannot subsist without outside intervention.</p>
<p><strong>Emergency</strong> is a state in which normal procedures are suspended and extra-ordinary measures are taken in order to avert a disaster. An emergency can be defined in the context of the social, political and epidemiological circumstances in which it occurs.</p>
<p>Hazard is a rare or extreme event in the natural or human made environment that adversely affects human life, property or activity to the extent of causing a disaster. It is essential to make a distinction between hazards and disasters, and to recognize that the effect of the former upon the latter is essentially a measure of the society's vulnerability.</p>
<p><strong>Mitigation:</strong> is permanent reduction of the risk of a disaster. Primary mitigation refers to reducing the resistance of the hazard and reducing vulnerability. Secondary mitigation refers to reducing the effects of the hazard (preparedness).</p>
<p>Mitigation includes recognizing that disasters will occur; attempts are made to reduce the harmful effects of a disaster and to limit their impact on human suffering and economic assets.</p>
<p><strong>Prevention</strong> is defined as those activities taken to prevent a natural phenomenon or potential hazard from having harmful effects on either people or economic assets.</p>
<p>Delayed actions drain the economy and the resources for emergency response within a region. For developing nations, prevention is perhaps the most critical components in managing disasters, however, it is clearly one of the most difficult to promote.</p>
<p>Prevention planning is based on two issues: hazard identification (identifying the actual threats facing a community) and vulnerability assessment (evaluating the risk and capacity of a community to handle the consequences of the disaster).</p>
<p>This document is available on

<p>Once these issues put in order of priority, emergency managers can determine the appropriate prevention strategies.</p>
<p>Disaster prevention refers to measures taken to eliminate the root-causes that make people vulnerable to disaster</p>
<p><strong>Preparedness</strong>: Are the measures that ensure the organized mobilization of personnel, funds, equipments, and supplies within a safe environment for effective relief. Disaster preparedness is building up of capacities before a disaster situation prevails in order to reduce impacts. Its measures include inter alia, availability of food reserve, emergency reserve fund, seed reserve, health facilities, warning systems, logistical infrastructure, relief manual, and shelves of projects (13). Reconstruction: the full resumption of socioeconomic activities and preventive measures.</p>
<p><strong>Rehabilitation</strong>: the restoration of the basic social functions.</p>
<p><strong>Resilience</strong>: is adaptability, capacity to recovery</p>
<p><strong>Response</strong>: is the set of activities implemented after the impact of a disaster in order to assess the needs, reduce the suffering, limit the spread and the consequences of the disaster, open the way to rehabilitation.</p>
<p>Risk is the expected losses (lives lost, persons injured, damages to property and disruption of economic activity) due to a particular hazard. Risk is the product of hazard and vulnerability. Risk is the probability that a person will experience an event in a specified period of time. Risk as a function of hazard and vulnerability, a relationship that is frequently illustrated with the following formula, although the association is not strictly asthmatic</p>
<p>Risk = hazard  vulnerability.</p>
<p>Risk is the probability of being affected by the unwanted consequences of a hazard. It combines the level of hazard and degree of vulnerability. Risk assessment is a term used widely for a systematic approach to characterizing the risks posed to individuals and populations by potentially adverse exposures. Susceptibility: is exposure to danger.</p>
<p><strong>Vulnerability</strong>: is the degree of loss resulting from a potentially damaging phenomenon</p>
<p>It is the susceptibility of a population to specific type of event. Vulnerability is also associated with the degree of possible or potential loss from a risk that results from a hazard at a given intensity.</p>
<p>The factors that influence intensity include demographics, the age, and resilience of the environment, technology, social differentiation and diversity as well as regional and global economics and politics. It is essential to make distinction between hazard and disaster.</p>
<p>The presence of hazard by its self can neither cause risk nor disasters. There are different <strong>predisposing factors</strong> that make vulnerable the survivors. The following are some of such underlying causes:</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Poverty: Virtually all disaster studies show that the wealthiest of the population survive the disaster, remains unaffected or are able to recover quickly.</p>
<p>Population growth: Increasing number of people will compete for limited amount of resources which can lead to conflict and conflict may result in crisis-induced migration.</p>
<p>Rapid urbanization: competition for scarce resources is an inevitable consequence of rapid urbanization, leading to human-made disasters.</p>
<p>Transitions in cultural practices: Societies are constantly changing and in continuous state of transition. These transitions are often extremely disruptive and uneven, leaving gaps in social coping mechanisms and technology.</p>
<p>Conflicts as well as transitional cultural practices can also Disaster Prevention and Preparedness lead to civil conflict (e.g. communal violence triggered by religious differences).</p>
<p>Environmental degradation: Deforestation leads to rapid rain runoff, which contributes to flooding.</p>
<p>Lack of awareness and information: Disasters can also happen because people vulnerable to them simply do not know how to get out of harm's way or to take protective measures.</p>
<p>Wars and civil strife are among the principal man made factors precipitating disaster. Disaster mainly occurs when vulnerability and hazard meet. The</p>
<p>Disaster Prevention and Preparedness</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="types-of-disasters">TYPES OF DISASTERS<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#types-of-disasters" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Disasters are broadly divided into two types comprising natural and man-made (technological) disasters.</p>
<p>There is also other basis by which disasters can be categorized which has been mentioned later in this section.</p>
<p>Natural disasters: Natural disasters occur as the result of action of the natural forces and tend to be accepted as unfortunate, but inevitable.</p>
<p>The natural disasters result from forces of climate and geology. Natural disasters are perhaps the most "unexpected" and costly overall in terms of loss of human lives and resources.</p>
<p>Natural disasters can be categorized as "acute" or "slow" in their onset. They should be predictable in certain instances since they cluster in geographical areas.</p>
<p>Natural disasters with acute onsets include events such as earthquake, flood, hurricane, cyclone or typhoon, tornado, fire, tsunami or storm surge, avalanche, volcanic eruption, extreme cold or blizzard, and heat wave.</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>5</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Natural hazards with slow or gradual onset include drought, famine, desertification, deforestation, and pest infestation.</p>
<p>Among the different types of natural disasters, floods, tropical storms, droughts and earth-quakes are the most destructive followed by landslide and storms.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="human-technological-causes">Human (technological) causes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#human-technological-causes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The technological or manmade disasters result from some human activities, such as explosions, fires, the release of toxic chemicals or radioactive materials, bridge or building collapse, crashes, dam or levee failure, nuclear reactor accidents, breaks in water, gas, or sewer lines, deforestation, war, etc.</p>
<p>Technological disasters tend to involve many more casualties than natural disasters of the same magnitude of energy release.</p>
<p>Technological or man-made disasters are unpredictable, can spread across geographical areas, may be unpreventable and may have limited physical damage but long-term effect.</p>
<p>They are also much more difficult for the community to deal with and for victims to accept. In technological disasters, there are issues of blame involved and the community spends much time discussing who was responsible and what mistakes were made.</p>
<p>Increasingly, agencies involved in disasters and their management are concerned with the interactions between man and nature, which can be complex and can aggravate disasters.</p>
<p>Communities in which industrial sites are located or through which hazardous material pass via high way, rail, or pipeline are at risk for technological disasters. Injuries can occur to workers at the site, to responders bringing the incident under control and providing emergency medical care, and to residents in the community. Those with pre-existing medical conditions, such as lung or heart diseases, could be at increased risk for negative health outcomes if exposed to toxic related skin disorders, and lung damage can result from exposure to specific agents. Ensuring that local industry implements basic safety procedures can significantly reduce negative health outcome from accidental release of toxins.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="another-classification-of-disasters-will-be-as-follows">Another classification of disasters will be as follows:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#another-classification-of-disasters-will-be-as-follows" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Sudden-onset disasters include floods, earthquakes, tsunamis, or tidal waves, tropical storms, volcanic eruptions, and landslides. As their name implies, sudden-onset disasters occur swiftly and often without any warning. Floods are the most frequent type of natural disaster associated with sudden migration of large populations and food shortages. Other types of disasters generally occur more frequently in Asia, Latin America, and the Caribbean rather than in Africa. When these disasters occur, they frequently cause thousands of deaths and casualties. Earthquakes cause the greatest number of deaths and overwhelming infrastructural damage. Communities at risk of these types of disasters should recognize and respond to threats posed by local weather patterns and the shape and contours of the land.</li>
</ol>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>
<p>Slow-onset disasters include droughts, famine, environmental degradation, deforestation (loss of trees and vegetation), pest infestation and desertification (conversion of arable lands to deserts). These disasters are usually the result of adverse weather conditions combined with poor land use.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Industrial/technological disasters result from a society's industrial and technological activities that lead to pollution, spillage, explosions, and fires. They may occur because of poor planning and construction of manmade facilities (buildings, factories, etc.) or from neglect of safety procedures. Sudden-onset disasters such as earthquakes, floods, and terrorist acts may trigger secondary disasters such as fires or pollution. Industrial events have the potential to cause large-scale loss of life and infrastructural damage, especially in developing countries with unregulated industrialization, and inadequate safety standards and disaster response capacity. Wherever there is a man-made facility, there is the potential for an industrial or technological disaster to occur. Reducing the occurrence and effects of industrial disasters requires a multi-sectoral approach.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Complex emergencies are usually man-made, with multiple contributing factors. They often follow wars between states, internal conflict, and, increasingly, terrorist acts. Massive population displacements may occur due to lack of food, insecurity, and increasing death rates. Poverty and risk of conflict go hand in hand</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>Epidemic diseases are those diseases that normally do not occur in stable communities but have the potential to spread under certain conditions. This can cause frequent and severe outbreaks. These diseases may be spread by contaminated water or food, person-to-person contact, or through animals or insect vectors. Examples of epidemic diseases that commonly threaten displaced populations include cholera, measles, dysentery, respiratory infections, malaria, and, increasingly, HIV. After a major disaster, the risk of epidemic diseases increases mainly as a result of overcrowding and unhygienic conditions</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="phases-of-disasters">Phases of Disasters<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#phases-of-disasters" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Disaster situations are dynamic, always changing and demanding a change in response. Disasters can be viewed as a series of phases on a time continuum. Even though the evolving situation may appear continuous, identifying and understanding these phases helps to describe related needs and to conceptualize causes related to disaster.</p>
<ol>
<li>
<p>Pre-emergency phase: the period before the disaster strikes may be used to assess how often a particular community is exposed to different risks (risk mapping) and how good is their preparedness. Protective actions can be undertaken based on the disaster warning and the available resources, e.g. identifying temporary shelters Disaster Prevention and Preparedness 19stocking basic supplies, planning evacuation routes, monitoring trends, etc.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Impact and flight phase: when a disaster strikes the hazard (fire, earthquake, floods, conflicts, etc) may trigger the displacement of large number of people from their homes. How long this phase lasts depends on the type of disaster, the number people affected and the distance they must travel to find</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>5</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>sanctuary. Search and rescue and other assistance, e.g. providing transportation, shelter and basic supplies to affected people may be carried out to reduce the loss of lives.</p>
<ol start="3">
<li>
<p>Acute phase: this phase begins immediately after the impact of the disaster and marked by intense, often reactive activities by many humanitarian agencies responding to media reports of very high death rate (may be 5-60 times the normal death rate). The priority is to keep the affected people alive. Security may be a major concern in complex emergency situations. Critical services such as providing food, water, sanitation, basic health care, protection from violence and harassment are to be quickly organized. This phase ends when crude death rate falls below 1/10,000 persons per day (the crude mortality rate for stable population in Africa is about 0.5/10,000 individual per day).</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Post emergency phase: the population movement usually slows down. This enables critical services to be properly established and maintained. During this phase the cause of displacement may be evolving. The displaced people wait for "something to happen so Disaster Prevention and Preparedness that they can return home, integrate with the local community or relocate elsewhere. As international support declines, the emphasis shifts to building local capacity of and promoting community participation. Tracing reproductive health care, mental health care and other services may be initiated, based on the epidemiological information. It is difficult to set time limits on the post-disaster time phases or to accurately define the limits of each, even for one specific type of disaster. For example, the emergency phase of a hurricane or a flood may be only a few days, or as long as a week. A drought and a resulting famine can last for months or even a year or more.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Repatriation phase: after the emergency situation is over, displaced people are expected to return to their place of origin either on their own or with the help of relief agencies. Repatriation may be either forced or voluntary.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Rehabilitation or reconstruction phase: once permanent solution is obtained, the focus shifts from relief to development. The aim is to help the affected community become self-reliant. The responsibility of providing assistance is handed over to the affected community, the local authorities, development agencies and other non-governmental organizations. Because humanitarian and development technicians have different approaches to providing assistance and the infrastructure for relief is inadequate for development, the programs are re-oriented and redesigned.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="exercise">Exercise<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#exercise" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="impact-of-disasters-on-the-health-system">Impact of Disasters on the Health System<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#impact-of-disasters-on-the-health-system" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>All forms of disasters and hazards affect human life. They impact on, and can at times compromise public health infrastructure and systems. What may first result in direct injuries and death may rapidly change to excess indirect illness and subsequent death as essential public health resources are destroyed, deteriorate, or are systematically denied to vulnerable populations.</p>
<p>The public health consequences include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Death</li>
<li> Injuries</li>
<li>Public concern for safety</li>
<li> Panic</li>
<li> Increases pests vectors</li>
<li> Worsening chronic illness</li>
<li> Loss of clean water</li>
<li> Loss of shelter</li>
<li> Loss of sanitation</li>
<li> Loss of routine hygiene</li>
<li> Damage to health care system</li>
<li> Toxic hazardous exposure</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="disaster-management-cycle">DISASTER MANAGEMENT CYCLE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#disaster-management-cycle" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Disaster management is a cyclical process. The end of one phase is the beginning of another, although one phase of the cycle does not necessarily have to be completed in order for the next to take place. Often, several phases take place concurrently. Timely decision making during each phase results in greater preparedness, better warnings, reduced vulnerability and or the prevention of future disasters.</p>
<p>A complete disaster management cycle includes the shaping of public policies and plans that either addresses the causes of disasters or mitigates their effects on people, property, and infrastructure.</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>5</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260111-be1e775a.jpeg" alt="img-1.jpeg"></p>
<p>Mitigation- Measures put in place to minimize the results from a disaster e.g. building codes and zoning, vulnerability analyses and public education.</p>
<p>Preparedness- Planning how to respond e.g. preparedness plans, emergency exercises/training and early warning systems.</p>
<p>Response- Initial actions taken as the event takes place. It involves efforts to minimize the hazards created by a disaster e.g. evacuation, search and rescue, and emergency relief.</p>
<p>Recovery- Returning the community to normal. Ideally, the affected area should be put in a condition equal to or better than it was before the disaster took place e.g. temporary housing, financial support and medical care.</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="module-competence">Module Competence<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#module-competence" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This module is designed to enable the learner acquire knowledge to identity and manage disasters.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="module-units">Module units<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#module-units" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Introduction to disaster management = 10 hours</li>
<li>Disaster management cycle = 30 hours</li>
<li>Education and public awareness at various levels 20 hours</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="module-outcomes">Module outcomes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#module-outcomes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>

<ol>
<li>Demonstrate the understanding of disaster management</li>
<li>Discuss the disaster management cycle</li>
<li>Conduct health education on disaster management</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="module-content">Module Content<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#module-content" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>Introduction to disaster management</strong>: Definition, types, implications, hazard identification and vulnerability analysis, the impact of disasters on development programmes, vulnerabilities caused by development.</p>
<p><strong>Disaster management cycle</strong>: preparedness, response, recovery and mitigation.</p>
<p><strong>Education on disaster management</strong>: Rationale for a Community Based Approach, stakeholder's roles and responsibilities, duties of response personnel, hazardous materials, understanding and appreciating culture and its distribution to education</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="terminologies">TERMINOLOGIES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#terminologies" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Disaster Management can be defined as the organization and management of resources and responsibilities for dealing with all humanitarian aspects of emergencies, in particular preparedness, response and recovery in order to lessen the impact of disasters.</p>
<p>KMTC</p>
<p>ISO 9001:2015 Certified</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Birosoft Enterprises (<a href="mailto:birosoftenterprises@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">birosoftenterprises@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260111-c9d14383.jpeg" alt="img-7.jpeg"></p>
<p>The first people to respond to a disaster are those living in the local community. They are the first to start rescue and relief operations. The Red Cross and Red Crescent National Societies therefore focus on community-based <strong>disaster preparedness</strong>, which assists community to <strong>reduce their vulnerability</strong> to disasters and strengthen their capacities to resist them.</p>
<p>When the capacity of a community or country to <strong>respond</strong> and <strong>recover</strong> from a disaster is overwhelmed, and upon request from the National Society, the International Federation uses its regional and international networks, assets and resources to bring assistance to the community and National Red Cross Red Crescent Society which is assisting them. At an International level, the International Federation advocates with Governments, international organizations and humanitarian donors for better practice and accountability in disaster management and greater respect of the dignity of the vulnerable people.</p>
<p><strong>According to Oxford Dictionary</strong>, a crisis is a decisive momenta time of great difficulty, a disaster, or a catastrophe. It is a turning point that changes the destiny of an individual or a group or a company or a government.</p>
<p>Crisis and Disaster are used as synonymous. However, usually crisis is considered as manmade and a disaster as a natural calamity.</p>
<p>KMTC
ISO 9001:2015 Certified 4
Downloaded by Birosoft Enterprises (<a href="mailto:birosoftenterprises@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">birosoftenterprises@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>World Bank, 2001; Defines disaster; as a sudden, extremely unfortunate event that affects many people to the scale that cannot be managed by the Local population.</p>
<p>A disaster can be defined as any occurrence that causes damage, ecological disruption, loss of human life or deterioration of health and health services on a scale sufficient to warrant an extraordinary response from outside the affected community or area (3).</p>
<p>It includes natural occurrences such as earthquake, volcanic eruptions, floods, famine and so on. It also includes man made calamities such as bomb blasts, accidents, looting and rioting during communal riots, etc.</p>
<p>Disaster management is like taking medicine for a disease. It has equal importance like medicine for a disease.</p>
<p>Disaster may be a natural calamity, epidemic diseases, industrial failures like bhopal gas tragedy, fukoshima nuclear power plant disaster etc.</p>
<p>All of these are very dangerous to human life and wipe out millions in a flash period.</p>
<p>Disaster management is about both aftermath management and precautionary measures before the disaster. India has many seismically vulnerable zones (earthquake zones), mountain regions (landslides), large rivers (flash floods), long coastline vulnerable to tsunamis and cyclonic. To face these disasters, we need to have a mechanism and that is disaster management.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="disaster-management">Disaster Management:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#disaster-management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Is more than just response and relief (i.e., it assumes a more proactive approach)</p>
<p>Is a systematic process (i.e., is based on the key management principles of planning, organizing, and leading which includes coordinating and controlling)</p>
<p>Aims to reduce the negative impact or consequences of adverse events (i.e., disasters cannot always be prevented, but the adverse effects can be minimized). It is a system with many components.</p>
<p>Hazard: Is the potential for a natural or human-caused event to occur with negative consequences (key words) A hazard can become an emergency; when the emergency moves beyond the control of the population, it becomes a disaster.</p>
<p>Emergency: Is a situation generated by the real or imminent occurrence of an event that requires immediate attention (key words) Paying immediate attention to an event or situation as described above is important as the event/situation can generate negative consequences and escalate into an emergency. The purpose of planning is to minimize those consequences.</p>
<p>Disaster: Is a natural or human-caused event which causes intensive negative impacts on people, goods, services and/or the environment, exceeding the affected communitys capability to respond (key words)</p>
<p>KMTC</p>
<p>ISO 9001:2015 Certified</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>5</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Birosoft Enterprises (<a href="mailto:birosoftenterprises@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">birosoftenterprises@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Risk: "Is the probability that loss will occur as the result of an adverse event, given the hazard and the vulnerability" (key words) Risk (R) can be determined as a product of hazard (H) and vulnerability (V). i.e. R = H x V</p>
<p>Vulnerability: "Is the extent to which a community's structure, services or environment is likely to be damaged or disrupted by the impact of a hazard" (key words)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="types-of-vulnerability-and-their-characteristics">Types of Vulnerability and their Characteristics<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#types-of-vulnerability-and-their-characteristics" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Tangible/Material (easy to see; value easily determined);</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>People  lives, health, security, living conditions</li>
<li>Property  services, physical property loss, loss of use</li>
<li>Economy  loss of products and production, income</li>
<li>Environment  water, soil, air, vegetation, wildlife</li>
</ul>
<ol start="2">
<li>Intangible/Abstract (difficult to see; value difficult to determine)</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Social structures  family and community relationships</li>
<li>Cultural practices  religious and agricultural</li>
<li>Cohesion  disruption of normal life</li>
<li>Motivation  will to recover; government response</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="factors-contributing-to-vulnerability">Factors Contributing to Vulnerability<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#factors-contributing-to-vulnerability" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>Poverty; People who are already in a depressed state are less able to recover. Some people are even more vulnerable  pregnant women, children and the disabled.</li>
<li>Population growth; Population has grown dramatically over the past decade</li>
<li>Rapid urbanization; Growing concentration around the capital. For example, two-thirds of the Nairobi population lives in slums.</li>
<li>Transition in cultural practices; Increase in sub-standard housing in more heavily populated urban areas.</li>
<li>Changes in traditional coping mechanisms; Declines in self-reliance, food conservation and preservation, warning systems etc.</li>
<li>Environmental degradation; As resources are consumed, vegetation cover removed, water polluted and air fouled, a country is more vulnerable to a disaster.</li>
<li>Lack of awareness and information; When people and government officials are unaware or lack information about disaster management, they fail to take appropriate actions.</li>
<li>Civil Strife and unrest; Resources are consumed, people are in a stressed situation, and transportation is restricted.</li>
<li>Geographical isolation; Island countries are disadvantaged by their relative remoteness, particular their limited access to schools, health and cash.</li>
<li>High disaster impact; Limited economies (tourism, agriculture). Disaster impact can affect an entire economy.</li>
<li>Political uncertainties/instability; Changing government policies, changing personnel in the national focal point, economic weakness all can contribute to an effective national disaster management programme.</li>
</ol>
<p>KMTC
ISO 9001:2015 Certified
Downloaded by Birosoft Enterprises (<a href="mailto:birosoftenterprises@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">birosoftenterprises@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>An emergency and a disaster are two different situations:</p>
<p>An <em>emergency</em> is a situation in which the community is capable of coping. It is a situation generated by the real or imminent occurrence of an event that requires immediate attention and that requires immediate attention of emergency resources.</p>
<p>A <em>disaster</em> is a situation in which the community is incapable of coping. It is a natural or human-caused event which causes intense negative impacts on people, goods, services and/or the environment, exceeding the affected communitys capability to respond; therefore the community seeks the assistance of government and international agencies.</p>
<p>The importance of disaster management lies in the management.</p>
<p>The presence of a disaster is merely the stimulus for capable managers to lead themselves and their organization from a bad place to a better place in order to survive and thrive AFTER the disaster. That is the WHY and the WHAT. HOW then follows.</p>
<p>The HOW is a little more complex but MUCH more manageable with training and a mindset of response leadership.</p>
<p>Disasters cannot be prevented entirely, but their impact can be reduced by the way they are managed.</p>
<p>If you have ever jumped into water that was deep enough that you could not touch the bottom of the environment, you will recognize that you are going to be at the mercy of that environment for a limited amount of time. Even very skillful swimmers will sink below the surface where the bubbles formed by their entry make the water temporarily unstable. Once downward momentum has been overcome, the swimmer is free to begin moving and his/her movement becomes more effective as the water settles.</p>
<p>If you were standing on the deck of a ship that collided with another ship, knocking you and many other people into the water at the same time, you would be moving a step closer toward a disaster. People are falling into the water at different intervals, falling rather than jumping, some are injured by their impact with the water, and others were injured before they fell so that they entered the water impaired by what preceded the fall, while others fall on top of one another. You can no longer take it for granted that any given direction is safe to move in. Weaker swimmers might cling to you or even try to climb out of the water, using you as their flotation device.</p>
<p>What characterizes a disaster is that the event overwhelms the capacity for response within the area in which the disaster occurs. These two ships are taking on water and they will soon sink. One has caught fire from spilled fuel. There is general confusion, people are panicking, equipment and debris are falling around you as lifeboats are being lowered, and you cannot return to the ship because it is no longer a safe place to be. Moreover, the ship is beginning to tilt toward you so that you cannot remain close to it for shelter because it may roll over you as it</p>
<p>KMTC
ISO 9001:2015 Certified
This document is available on
studocu
Downloaded by Birosoft Enterprises (<a href="mailto:birosoftenterprises@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">birosoftenterprises@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>sinks. Being far from land, you must determine in which direction you should swim as well as how far you should go in order to be safe, but not exhausted.</p>
<p>Disaster management suggests that the ships had meaningful plans in place, their crews were trained, they had an adequate number of life vests and life boats, and they communicated their situation to others outside of the affected area so that they could receive appropriate aid.</p>
<p>Your business, your community, and your government agencies should have determined the most likely forms that a disaster could take and have made their plans accordingly. They know what they expect people to do and what they will need in order to do it as soon as a disaster asserts itself. This gives them some element of control over the event. That element of control provides the best chances to save lives and to protect property.</p>
<p>Without disaster management, everyone is a victim of the event and the myriad reactions that others have to the event. With it, people have a way in which they can improve their chances for their personal and collective survival, protect property, protect vital resources, and recover both faster and more completely.</p>
<p>On a very practical level, insurers will not pay claims for damages that could have been prevented. Another incentive is that no one wants to say they knew lives might be lost and property might be destroyed, but they did not consider those things important enough to warrant their attention. Media focus on the event would shift to the sensational reporting of any lapses in the rush to fix blame on those who were responsible for the general wellbeing and had failed in their duties.</p>
<p>In any country, in any organization, in any building, in any premises it's important to get prepared for a worst situation. In situation of a disaster, most of the people loose their ability to think straight, they panic. In panic, it's obvious that wrong steps would be taken out of fear, it's a chaotic situation that leads to wrong moves adding to the damage of disaster. It's important to do right things that would salvage as much possible. Hence, it is advised to plan moves like a drill, to minimize damage, to take right steps. Planning of this drill is disaster management. This not only manages people in move but get a place better equipped with necessary objects in a right place.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="importance-of-disaster-management">IMPORTANCE OF DISASTER MANAGEMENT<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#importance-of-disaster-management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>To avert a disaster</li>
</ol>
<p>Disaster management teams can help to avert a disaster before it occurs. The Disaster management team may examine the possible causes of disaster, and may take appropriate measures to avert a disaster. For instance, forest fires, or even terrorists bombings can be averted through effective planning and pre-emptive action.</p>
<ol start="2">
<li>To undertake rescue operations: -</li>
</ol>
<p>Disaster management personnel can undertake rescue operations effectively. Trained disaster</p>
<p>KMTC</p>
<p>ISO 9001:2015 Certified 8</p>
<p>Downloaded by Birosoft Enterprises (<a href="mailto:birosoftenterprises@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">birosoftenterprises@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>management personnel can rescue people effectively at the time of floods, major fires, building collapses, and so on</p>
<p>To provide relief measures:</p>
<p>Disaster management team is responsible to provide relief measures to the victims. For instance, the team can make arrangement for food, clothing, and relief camps, medicines and so on. Such measures would reduce the misery of the disaster victims.</p>
<ol start="4">
<li>To undertake rehabilitation programmes:</li>
</ol>
<p>Disaster management team can work effectively to undertake rehabilitation programmes in the affected areas. For instance, in the earthquake affected areas, rehabilitation programmes include:</p>
<p>a. Construction of dwellings
b. Schools and other infrastructures</p>
<ol start="5">
<li>To undertake liaison work:</li>
</ol>
<p>The disaster management team undertakes liaison work relating to the disaster. The liaison work is required with various agencies-private and government (including hospitals) in order to obtain funds and donations, and other resources or services so as to manage and overcome the disaster.</p>
<ol start="6">
<li>To reduce trauma and tension:</li>
</ol>
<p>The Disaster management team can help to reduce the trauma and tension before and after the disaster. For instance, before a disaster, the team can properly guide the people to face or handle the disaster such as floods. Also, after the disaster, the team can provide not only material or financial support, but also psychological support to overcome the traumatic effect of disaster.</p>
<ol start="7">
<li>To protect the Environment:</li>
</ol>
<p>Disaster management team can help to protect and preserve the environment. For example, a disaster management team can plan pre-emptive action to avert forest fires Etc</p>
<ol start="8">
<li>To minimize losses</li>
</ol>
<p>Disaster management teams can help to minimize loss of life and property. This is because; the Disaster management team can take pre-emptive actions to avert a disaster.</p>
<p>If a disaster should ever occur, when proper preparations are made, dealing with the disaster - in other words, managing the situation - is made more timely and efficient than if there were no disaster management plan. Anticipating the possibility of certain types of disasters can lead to a reduction in the vulnerability of the community to such an event, as well as planning for what will be needed should a disaster occur. If no planning has been done and management of the event is based on "spur of the moment" decisions without any clear organization of the process, it's likely that recovery from the disaster will be delayed. That can mean more human fatalities and more cost. That seems important enough.</p>
<p>KMTC</p>
<p>ISO 9001:2015 Certified 9</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="types-of-natural-and-non-natural-disasters">Types of natural and non-natural disasters<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#types-of-natural-and-non-natural-disasters" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Disasters are often classified according to their:</p>
<ol>
<li>causes  natural vs. human</li>
<li>speed of onset  sudden vs. slow</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="a-causes">A. CAUSES<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#a-causes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="1-natural-disasters">1. Natural Disasters<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-natural-disasters" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>These types of disaster naturally occur in proximity to, and pose a threat to, people, structures or economic assets. They are caused by biological, geological, seismic, hydrologic, or meteorological conditions or processes in the natural environment (e.g., cyclones, earthquakes, tsunami, floods, landslides, and volcanic eruptions).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="human-made-disasters">Human-Made Disasters<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#human-made-disasters" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>These are disasters or emergency situations of which the principal, direct causes are identifiable human actions, deliberate or otherwise. Apart from technological disasters this mainly involves situations in which civilian populations suffer casualties, losses of property, basic services and means of livelihood as a result of war, civil strife or other conflicts, or policy implementation. In many cases, people are forced to leave their homes, giving rise to congregations of refugees or externally and/or internally displaced persons as a result of civil strife, an airplane crash, a major fire, oil spill, epidemic, terrorism, etc.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="b-speed-of-onset">B. SPEED OF ONSET<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#b-speed-of-onset" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>
<p><strong>Sudden onset</strong>: little or no warning, minimal time to prepare. For example, an earthquake, tsunami, cyclone, volcano, etc.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p><strong>Slow onset</strong>: adverse event slow to develop; first the situation develops; the second level is an emergency; the third level is a disaster. For example, drought, civil strife, epidemic, etc.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="generally-common-disasters-include">Generally, Common Disasters include<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#generally-common-disasters-include" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Epidemic</li>
</ul>

<ul>
<li>Earthquake</li>
<li>Fire</li>
<li>Flood</li>
<li>Cyber attack</li>
<li>Sabotage (insider or external threat)</li>
<li>Hurricane or other major storm</li>
<li>Power outage</li>
<li>Water outage (supply interruption, contamination)</li>
<li>Telecoms outage</li>
<li>IT outage</li>
<li>Terrorism/Piracy</li>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="implications-effects-of-disasters-on-the-community-and-environment">Implications / effects of disasters on the community and environment<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#implications-effects-of-disasters-on-the-community-and-environment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A disaster in any region has a devastating impact. The following list identifies a few of the unpleasant impacts:</p>
<ul>
<li>Infrastructure damage</li>
<li>Telecommunication loss</li>
<li>Flooding</li>
<li>Landslides</li>
<li>Power disruption</li>
<li>Water problems</li>
<li>Agricultural damage</li>
<li>Loss/damage to housing</li>
<li>Damage to inland and coastal environments</li>
<li>Disruption of standard of living, lifestyle, etc.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="impact-scenarios">Impact scenarios<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#impact-scenarios" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>After identifying the applicable threats, impact scenarios are considered to support the development of a business recovery plan. Business continuity testing plans may document scenarios for each identified threats and impact scenarios. More localized impact scenarios  for example loss of a specific floor in a building  may also be documented. The BC plans should reflect the requirements to recover the business in the widest possible damage. The risk assessment should cater to developing impact scenarios that are applicable to the business or the premises it operates. For example, it might not be logical to consider tsunami in the region of Mideast since the likelihood of such a threat is negligible.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="recovery-requirement">Recovery requirement<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#recovery-requirement" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>

<p>After the analysis phase, business and technical recovery requirements precede the solutions phase. Asset inventories allow for quick identification of deployable resources. For an office-based, IT-intensive business, the plan requirements may cover desks, human resources, applications, data, manual workarounds, computers and peripherals. Other business environments, such as production, distribution, warehousing etc. will need to cover these elements, but likely have additional issues.</p>
<p>The robustness of an emergency management plan is dependent on how much money an organization or business can place into the plan. The organization must balance realistic feasibility with the need to properly prepare. In general, every <span class="math math-inline"><span class="katex"><span class="katex-mathml"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML"><semantics><mrow><mn>1</mn><mi>p</mi><mi>u</mi><mi>t</mi><mi>i</mi><mi>n</mi><mi>t</mi><mi>o</mi><mi>a</mi><mi>n</mi><mi>e</mi><mi>m</mi><mi>e</mi><mi>r</mi><mi>g</mi><mi>e</mi><mi>n</mi><mi>c</mi><mi>y</mi><mi>m</mi><mi>a</mi><mi>n</mi><mi>a</mi><mi>g</mi><mi>e</mi><mi>m</mi><mi>e</mi><mi>n</mi><mi>t</mi><mi>p</mi><mi>l</mi><mi>a</mi><mi>n</mi><mi>w</mi><mi>i</mi><mi>l</mi><mi>l</mi><mi>p</mi><mi>r</mi><mi>e</mi><mi>v</mi><mi>e</mi><mi>n</mi><mi>t</mi></mrow><annotation encoding="application/x-tex">1 put into an emergency management plan will prevent </annotation></semantics></math></span><span class="katex-html" aria-hidden="true"><span class="base"><span class="strut" style="height: 0.8889em; <div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="solution-design">Solution design<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#solution-design" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The solution design phase identifies the most cost-effective disaster recovery solution that meets two main requirements from the impact analysis stage.</p>
<p>For IT purposes, this is commonly expressed as the minimum application and data requirements and the time in which the minimum application and application data must be available.</p>
<p>Outside the IT domain, preservation of hard copy information, such as contracts, skilled staff or restoration of embedded technology in a process plant must be considered. This phase overlaps with disaster recovery planning methodology.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-solution-phase-determines">The solution phase determines:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-solution-phase-determines" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>crisis management command structure</li>
<li>secondary work sites</li>
<li>telecommunication architecture between primary and secondary work sites</li>
<li>data replication methodology between primary and secondary work sites</li>
<li>applications and data required at the secondary work site</li>
<li>Physical data requirements at the secondary work site.</li>
</ul>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="emergency-response-teams">Emergency response teams<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#emergency-response-teams" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="emotional-and-physical-damage-after-emergencies">Emotional and physical damage after emergencies<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#emotional-and-physical-damage-after-emergencies" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Trauma model of mental disorders</li>
</ul>
<p>Many communities are susceptible to frequent severe earthquakes because of their geographical position and geological environment, while others do not experience them. Of the susceptible community, some, like San Francisco, and many community in Japan, are highly resilient and some, like Armenia, less resilient.</p>
<p>This difference in resilience can be due to:</p>
<ul>
<li>different abilities of buildings, and various elements of the infrastructure, to withstand seismic loads;</li>
<li>differences in emergency preparedness (i.e. the degree to which a community is organized to cope with emergencies);</li>
<li>the extent of the resources that can be applied to an emergency;</li>
<li>the degree to which the province or nation can sustain economic and social damage.</li>
</ul>
<p>The vulnerability of sections smaller and larger than a community, such as individual buildings, organizations, national economies, and political structures, can also be described in terms of susceptibility and resilience.</p>

ISO 9001:2015 Certified

<p>Vulnerability reduction requires a number of coordinated activities, including:</p>
<ul>
<li>Policy development;</li>
<li>Vulnerability assessment (to describe the problems and opportunities);</li>
<li>Emergency prevention and mitigation (to reduce susceptibility);</li>
<li>Emergency preparedness (to increase resilience).</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="self-assessment-task">Self-Assessment task<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#self-assessment-task" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ol>
<li>
<p>Define the following:
i. Disaster management;
ii. Hazard;
iii. Emergency;
iv. Disaster;
v. Vulnerability; and
vi. Risk</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Differentiate between an emergency and a disaster situation</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Identify and describe three natural disasters which you are familiar with; identify and describe three man-made disasters you have learnt about.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>List and describe one main hazard to which your community is vulnerable to.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Identify and briefly discuss one adverse impact that a particular disaster has left behind on your lifestyle and environment</p>
</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="disaster-management-cycle">Disaster Management Cycle<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#disaster-management-cycle" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="phase-i-mitigation-introduction">Phase I: Mitigation Introduction<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#phase-i-mitigation-introduction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>KMTC</p>
<p>ISO 9001:2015 Certified 14</p>
<p>Downloaded by Birosoft Enterprises (<a href="mailto:birosoftenterprises@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">birosoftenterprises@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Disaster management is an enormous task. They are not confined to any particular location, neither do they disappear as quickly as they appear.</p>
<p>Therefore, it is imperative that there is proper management to optimize efficiency of planning and response. Due to limited resources, collaborative efforts at the governmental, private and community levels are necessary. This level of collaboration requires a coordinated and organized effort to mitigate against, prepare for, respond to, and recover from emergencies and their effects in the shortest possible time.</p>
<p>Upon completion of this section, the student will be able to:</p>
<p>i. Define the various phases of the disaster management cycle.
ii. Explain the importance of disaster mitigation.
iii. Describe how disaster management can be integrated into public policy.
iv. Explain how planning and design of infrastructure should take into account the vulnerability of the community.</p>
<p><strong>Mitigation:</strong> Reducing or minimizing an impact of a hazard or disaster.</p>
<p><strong>Risk management:</strong> Consists of identifying threats (hazards likely to occur), determining their probability of occurrence, estimating what the impact of the threat might be to the community at risk, determining measures that can reduce the risk, and taking action to reduce the threat.</p>
<p><strong>Vulnerability:</strong> A condition wherein human settlements, buildings, agriculture, or human health are exposed to a disaster by virtue of their construction or proximity to hazardous terrain.</p>
<p>Disaster management is a cyclical process; the end of one phase is the beginning of another (see diagram below), although one phase of the cycle does not necessarily have to be completed in order for the next to take place. Often several phases are taking place concurrently. Timely decision making during each phase results in greater preparedness, better warnings, reduced vulnerability and/or the prevention of future disasters.</p>
<p>The complete disaster management cycle includes the shaping of public policies and plans that either addresses the causes of disasters or mitigates their effects on people, property, and infrastructure.</p>
<p>The mitigation and preparedness phases occur as improvements are made in anticipation of an event. By embracing development, a community's ability to mitigate against and prepare for a disaster is improved. As the event unfolds, disaster managers become involved in the immediate response and long-term recovery phases.</p>
<p>KMTC
ISO 9001:2015 Certified
This document is available on
s
studocu
Downloaded by Birosoft Enterprises (<a href="mailto:birosoftenterprises@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">birosoftenterprises@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p><img src="assets/images/image-20260111-17b7de36.jpeg" alt="img-8.jpeg">
Figure 2: Disaster Management Cycle</p>
<p>Mitigation: Measures put in place to minimize the results from a disaster.</p>
<p>Examples: building codes and zoning; vulnerability analyses; public education.</p>
<p>Preparedness: Planning how to respond. Examples: preparedness plans; emergency exercises/training; warning systems.</p>
<p>Response: Initial actions taken as the event takes place. It involves efforts to minimize the hazards created by a disaster. Examples: evacuation; search and rescue; emergency relief.</p>
<p>Recovery: Returning the community to normal. Ideally, the affected area should be put in a condition equal to or better than it was before the disaster took place. Examples: temporary housing; grants; medical care.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="disaster-mitigation">Disaster Mitigation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#disaster-mitigation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Mitigation refers to all actions taken before a disaster to reduce its impacts, including preparedness and long-term risk reduction measures.</p>
<p>Mitigation activities fall broadly into two categories:</p>
<ol>
<li>Structural mitigation  construction projects which reduce economic and social impacts</li>
<li>Non-structural activities  policies and practices which raise awareness of hazards or encourage developments to reduce the impact of disasters</li>
</ol>
<p>Mitigation includes reviewing building codes; vulnerability analysis updates; zoning and land-use management and planning; reviewing of building use regulations and safety codes; and implementing preventative health measures. (World Development Report, 1998)</p>
<p>KMTC</p>
<p>ISO 9001:2015 Certified</p>
<p>Downloaded by Birosoft Enterprises (<a href="mailto:birosoftenterprises@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">birosoftenterprises@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Mitigation can also involve educating businesses and the public on simple measures they can take to reduce loss or injury, for instance fastening bookshelves, water heaters, and filing cabinets to walls to keep them from falling during earthquakes. Ideally, these preventative measures and public education programmes will occur before the disaster.</p>
<p>The primary focus of disaster management is to prevent disasters wherever possible or to mitigate those which are inevitable.</p>
<p>Four sets of tools that could be used to prevent or mitigate disasters include:</p>
<p>i. Hazard management and vulnerability reduction
ii. Economic diversification
iii. Political intervention and commitment
iv. Public awareness</p>
<p>The first two apply exclusively to disasters caused by natural phenomena while the latter are used to mitigate any other hazards.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="mitigation-strategies">Mitigation strategies<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#mitigation-strategies" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Two aspects of mitigation include:</p>
<ol>
<li>Hazard identification and vulnerability analysis and</li>
<li>Various mitigation strategies or measures.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="mitigation-strategies-or-measures">Mitigation strategies or measures<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#mitigation-strategies-or-measures" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Adjusting normal development programmes to reduce losses.
For instance, varieties of crops that are more wind, flood or drought resistant can often be introduced in areas prone to floods, drought and cyclones.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Economic diversification. In areas where the principal or sole source of the income may be threatened, attempts should be made to diversify the economy and introduce the economic activities that are less vulnerable. Diversification is extremely important where economies are dependent on a single cash crop.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Developing disaster resistant economic activities. Some economic activities are relatively unaffected by disasters. For instance, situating warehouses in flood plains may be more appropriate than manufacturing plants in the same location. Coconut palms could be more suitable than other fruit trees in cyclone-prone coastal areas.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>Efforts should be made to identify and encourage the development of enterprises that are less vulnerable to the hazards.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="disaster-mitigation-and-infrastructure">Disaster Mitigation and Infrastructure<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#disaster-mitigation-and-infrastructure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Investment in infrastructure for the management of hydrological hazards  such as cyclones and floods  has significantly reduced the loss of life from an annual average of 100,000 persons during the past 50 years to 41,000 persons during the past 15 years. Investment in disaster management infrastructure falls into two categories:</p>
<ol>
<li>Investment in infrastructure to support sustainable socioeconomic development; and</li>
<li>Investment in infrastructure for reconstruction and recovery.</li>
</ol>
<p>Source: ESCAP, 1995. Asian and the Pacific Report on Natural Hazards and Natural Disaster Reduction.</p>
