<p>Vulnerability assessment</p>
<p>The value of vulnerability assessment
Vulnerability assessment (also known as hazard analysis, threat assessment, and risk assessment) is a procedure for identifying hazards and determining their possible effects on a community, activity, or organization.</p>
<p>It provides information essential for:
&gt; Sustainable development (because development will be undermined without programmes and strategies to reduce vulnerability);</p>
<p>KMTC
ISO 9001:2015 Certified 32
Downloaded by Birosoft Enterprises (<a href="mailto:birosoftenterprises@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">birosoftenterprises@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>&gt; Prevention, mitigation, and preparedness (without knowledge of what is likely to go wrong, and what the effects will be, it is impossible to be effectively prepared and difficult to prevent problems);
&gt; Emergency response (many emergencies seriously disrupt transportation and communications, and information may become either unreliable or non-existent; vulnerability assessment will suggest where most of the damage might occur);
&gt; Emergency recovery (vulnerability assessment can provide a baseline that describes the prior condition of the community, against which the effectiveness of recovery work can be compared).</p>
<p>There are numerous ways of assessing vulnerability. The process described in this manual consists of a series of steps, each containing a number of techniques.</p>
<p>Some of these steps are hazard identification, community and environmental analysis, and hazard description. In turn, there are techniques for identifying hazards, for describing the people, property, and environment they may affect, and for describing hazards.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-meaning-of-vulnerability">The meaning of "vulnerability"<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-meaning-of-vulnerability" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Vulnerability is the result of a number of factors that increase the chance that a community will be unable to deal with an emergency. Not all sections of a community are vulnerable to hazards, but most are vulnerable to some degree.</p>
<p>Vulnerability comprises two aspects  susceptibility and resilience.</p>
<p>Susceptibility concerns the factors operating in a community that allow a hazard to cause an emergency; examples of such factors range from a community's level of development to its location in an earthquake-prone area.</p>
<p>Resilience is the community's ability to withstand the damage caused by emergencies and disasters; it is a function of the various factors that allow a community to respond to and recover from emergencies.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="vulnerability-assessment">VULNERABILITY ASSESSMENT<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#vulnerability-assessment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Community and the individuals of which they are composed have a number of general needs during and after emergencies, of which personal safety is the most obvious. Thus, caring for the injured and protecting people from further harm are of paramount importance, together with providing food, drink, and shelter.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-meaning-of-hazard">The meaning of "hazard"<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-meaning-of-hazard" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>KMTC</p>
<p>ISO 9001:2015 Certified</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Birosoft Enterprises (<a href="mailto:birosoftenterprises@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">birosoftenterprises@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>In emergency management, "hazard" has various definitions. This "hazard" is any phenomenon that has the potential to cause disruption or damage to people and their environment.</p>
<p>Other definitions within the context of emergency management include:</p>
<ul>
<li>a threat to people and what they value;</li>
<li>a threat to life, well-being, material goods, and environment from the extremes of natural processes and technology;</li>
<li>potential for an agent or process to do harm.</li>
</ul>
<p>Common to all these definitions is the potential for harm.</p>
<p>A hazard is not an event</p>
<ul>
<li>it is the potential for an event. Thus "flood", in a general sense or even as applied to a particular place, is a hazard. An actual flood is an "incident", "emergency", or "disaster", depending on the damage it causes or how well it is managed.</li>
</ul>
<p>A vulnerability assessment relating to a flood in a particular community examines the potential for flood events and the possible effects of such events.</p>
<p>Analysing a flood that has occurred is not a vulnerability assessment because each event is unique, and emergency management based on a specific event will contain too many unjustified assumptions. What is true of one flood will not necessarily be true of another.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="community-environments-and-hazards">Community, environments, and hazards<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#community-environments-and-hazards" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In terms of emergency management, a community has an intimate relationship with its environment and its hazards. The following example illustrates how a community interacts with its environment and hazards. People move into a hilly, forested area, with a medium to high annual rainfall, and clear land for agriculture.</p>
<p>Before the forest was cleared, rainfall was absorbed and trapped by vegetation and accumulated humus before it could run off into creeks and rivers.</p>
<p>Forests and many other natural ecosystems, such as swamps, act as sponges during heavy rain by storing water, some of which then evaporates or is released slowly. While there is still the potential for flood, only the heaviest rain will cause serious flooding. Once a forested area is cleared, drainage characteristics change. There is less vegetation and humus to collect and store water, so the water runoff is greater. There is also more erosion, and the profile of the river bed is altered by the accumulation of silt.</p>
<p>When heavy rainfall occurs, therefore, there is a greater likelihood of serious flooding, which may lead, in turn, to further changes in the drainage system.</p>
<p>If part of this community lives on or near flood plains, and the settlement took account only of the flood characteristics that existed before forest clearing, problems might occur, including:</p>
<ul>
<li>severe floods leading to loss of life and destruction of buildings, bridges, roads, dams, and livestock;</li>
<li>removal of valuable topsoil from agricultural areas and, hence, decreasing productivity;</li>
<li>river siltation, leading to changes in the navigability of the river.</li>
</ul>
<p>Thus, changes in the environment caused by the community alter the hazards in the area, and the changed hazards may then have effects on the environment, the community, and the community's vulnerability. It is essential, therefore, to examine how the community, the environment, and the hazards interact when performing a vulnerability assessment.</p>
<p>Even normal population growth can increase vulnerability, by forcing community into marginal and hazardous areas.</p>
<p>KMTC
ISO 9001:2015 Certified
Downloaded by Birosoft Enterprises (<a href="mailto:birosoftenterprises@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">birosoftenterprises@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>The purpose of the process</p>
<p>There are several reasons why a rational process for assessing vulnerability is needed:
 to explain to others what is being done and how they can participate;
 to ensure that significant aspects of the vulnerability assessment are not missed;
 to justify the validity of the results, demonstrating that analysis has been thorough (this is particularly important when funding for emergency management is sought).</p>
<p>The steps in this process should be followed consecutively and information from each step used in subsequent ones.</p>
<p>Parts of the process</p>
<p>The parts of the vulnerability assessment process are as follows:</p>
<ol>
<li>The project definition determines the aim, objectives, scope, and context of the vulnerability assessment, the tasks to be performed, and the resources needed to perform them.</li>
<li>The formation of a representative planning group is essential to vulnerability assessment and emergency planning. Without this group it will be difficult to gather the required information, obtain the commitment of key individuals, and allow the community to participate.</li>
<li>Hazard identification reveals the hazards that exist in the community (although it is unlikely that all of the hazards will be discovered).</li>
<li>Hazard description presents the hazards that exist in the community. The same hazards may manifest themselves differently in different areas and community because there is an interaction between hazards, the particular community, and the environment.</li>
<li>A community and environment description outlines the relevant information about the people, property, or environment that may affect or be affected by the hazards. More hazards may be identified at this stage.</li>
<li>A description of effects is an account of community vulnerability  what is likely to happen in an accident, incident, emergency, or disaster involving a single hazard or multiple hazards.</li>
<li>Hazard prioritization determines the hazards that should be dealt with first, and those that can be dealt with later or ignored, on the basis of their likely effects and community vulnerability.</li>
<li>Recommendations for action are the link between vulnerability assessment and other emergency management activities. Planning, training and education, and monitoring and evaluation should be based firmly on the results of the vulnerability assessment.</li>
<li>Documentation of all results and decisions is necessary to justify the recommendations, and any further emergency preparedness work.</li>
</ol>
<p>Some problems in assessing vulnerability</p>
<p>Some of the common problems encountered in performing vulnerability assessments relate to data, lack of knowledge, attitudes, project scope, and rigid adherence to the process.</p>
<p>KMTC
ISO 9001:2015 Certified 35</p>
<p>This document is available on
studocu
Downloaded by Birosoft Enterprises (<a href="mailto:birosoftenterprises@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">birosoftenterprises@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>i. Data  Unavailability of data on some hazards; data of unknown reliability; too much data on other hazards; too much data on community and difficulty in determining which are relevant.
ii. Knowledge  Lack of knowledge in the community or the planning group of the methods of vulnerability assessment; lack of knowledge of specific hazards.
iii. Attitudes  A lack of cooperation from some agencies, particularly response agencies, who may perceive vulnerability assessment as an academic exercise only.
iv. Project scope  Poor definition of the area covered by the vulnerability assessment; trying to analyse too much; determining the amount of detail required in the hazard and community descriptions; presenting unrealistic and unachievable recommendations.
v. Rigid adherence to process  Forcing the hazard and community descriptions into too rigid a framework  some of the characteristics suggested in this manual will not work with some hazards and community; following the process in a lock-step manner and not allowing a return to earlier steps when required.
vi. Frustration with the process  It is possible that the planning group and the community will become frustrated with the vulnerability assessment process (problem analysis) and want to start emergency planning (problem-solving).</p>
<p>It may be possible to begin planning and implementing vulnerability reduction while a formal vulnerability assessment is being performed, but a complete vulnerability assessment remains desirable.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="alternative-processes">Alternative processes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#alternative-processes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The vulnerability assessment process described here  and the planning process described in Chapter 4  is one way of analysing and solving emergency preparedness problems. Another approach, which can be used to help start more formal vulnerability assessment and emergency planning, is the community needs and resource maps method.</p>
<p>This method encourages community participation at the grass roots by:</p>
<p>i. encouraging people to describe their problems;
ii. developing a preliminary list of community risks and needs;
iii. using field visits to verify risks and needs, and then creating maps that illustrate the problems and the available resources;
iv. Establishing a local committee to make plans to deal with the identified risks and needs.</p>
<p>While this method is useful, the formal processes of vulnerability assessment and emergency planning allow for a more accurate description of hazards and vulnerability and better emergency preparedness.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-planning-group">The planning group<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-planning-group" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="why-a-planning-group-is-necessary">Why a planning group is necessary<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#why-a-planning-group-is-necessary" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Once a project definition has been developed, forming a planning group is the second step in the vulnerability assessment process. Why is a planning group essential to the development of appropriate vulnerability assessments and emergency plans?</p>
<p>&gt; Firstly, rapid access to diverse information is essential. It is possible to gather this information through correspondence, interviews, and telephone calls, but this will take time. Assembling the people who can provide information will make information-gathering more efficient.</p>
<p>KMTC
ISO 9001:2015 Certified
Downloaded by Birosoft Enterprises (<a href="mailto:birosoftenterprises@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">birosoftenterprises@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>&gt; Secondly, no one is expert in everything and the contributions of experts in particular fields are required. Local experts may become the greatest critics of a vulnerability assessment if they are ignored.
&gt; Lastly, if the vulnerability assessment is to be taken seriously, the commitment of all relevant personnel is essential. Allowing people to contribute to the vulnerability assessment objectives and to work together towards a common goal are effective means of gaining this commitment.</p>
<p>Before a planning group is selected it is important to find out whether one already exists.</p>
<p>There may well be a group of people who are responsible for safety and crisis or emergency management in a given community. If such a group exists, it may be ideal for the purposes of preparing a vulnerability assessment because it may already have sufficient resources, the necessary authority, appropriate representation, an efficient reporting system, and sufficient expertise.</p>
<p>The suitability of any existing emergency management group should be assessed on the basis of the above criteria, as well as by reference to the project definition. Any shortcomings in the planning group will need to be addressed.</p>
<p>A planning group need not be fixed in its membership. The group will evolve according to its activities, and the end result will be a group of people committed to emergency preparedness in their community, who will be able to work together during actual emergencies.</p>
<p>However, changes in the planning group composition, although desirable, may cause problems. Groups of people who work together will often develop a strong sense of sectiony and newcomers may often find it difficult to win the groups acceptance or feel comfortable working with it. Understanding this potential problem is already a partial solution; group members must welcome new members and avoid forming barriers between themselves and the community.</p>
<p>The planning group may also serve as a formalized emergency committee. Each member must therefore have sufficient authority to represent his or her organization in preparedness and response.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="hazard-identification">Hazard identification<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#hazard-identification" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Hazard identification determines the hazards that may affect people in a community.</p>
<p>This third step in the vulnerability assessment process provides information for further analysis. Hazard identification is not straightforward  people may have quite different perceptions of what constitutes a significant hazard. For this reason seeking the views of a number of people in the community is essential.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="a-group-technique-for-identifying-hazards">A group technique for identifying hazards<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#a-group-technique-for-identifying-hazards" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Hazard identification should be undertaken by a group of people, such as a planning group, with expertise in the area of work and a commitment to the safety of that area. One quick method to determine peoples perceptions of the most serious hazards and avoid the pitfalls of groupthink,1 is the following:</p>
<ul>
<li>Each person in the group should be asked to write down the 10 hazards (in the area being investigated) that most concern them, and be given a few minutes to do this.</li>
<li>When they have finished the first task, they should rank, in terms of seriousness, the hazards they have listed as high, medium, and low (using their own definition of seriousness).</li>
</ul>
<p>KMTC
ISO 9001:2015 Certified
37
This document is available on
s
studocu
Downloaded by Birosoft Enterprises (<a href="mailto:birosoftenterprises@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">birosoftenterprises@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>Each person should then say what he or she has written down (without the ranking) and answers should be recorded on a blackboard, whiteboard, or large sheet of paper. Duplications should not be recorded; if very similar hazards are mentioned, planning group members should refine what they mean. Suggestions must not be belittled, but recorded uncritically.</li>
<li>When each person has contributed, a table similar to Table 5 should be drawn up.</li>
<li>Group members should be asked about each hazard listed; the numbers of people who consider each hazard to be high, medium, or low in seriousness should be recorded.</li>
</ul>
<p>The numbers recorded in the table indicate how people in the planning group feel about the hazards in the community and may reflect accurate knowledge on their part. The numbers certainly reflect the group's perception of which hazards are a problem. The numbers have no meaning outside the context of the planning group meeting and certainly should not be used for any other purposes.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="this-technique-has-the-following-benefits">This technique has the following benefits.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#this-technique-has-the-following-benefits" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>It allows everyone to have their say and avoids some of the problems of "groupthink". If everyone is allowed to contribute, the likelihood of developing a meaningful vulnerability assessment is greater.</li>
<li>It encourages interaction between people who may not know each other and may encourage all group members to continue contributing.</li>
<li>It prompts the members of the planning group to think analytically.</li>
<li>It demonstrates to all members of the group that people have divergent points of view concerning hazard and risk and will to some extent validate these different points of view.</li>
<li>It increases members' commitment to the vulnerability assessment because they have had a chance to contribute.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="other-techniques-for-identifying-hazards">Other techniques for identifying hazards<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#other-techniques-for-identifying-hazards" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Other techniques for identifying hazards include:</p>
<ul>
<li>researching the history of emergencies in the community, by consulting histories, newspapers, records, and older community members;</li>
<li>inspecting the community for evidence of previous emergencies, existing hazards, and existing vulnerability;</li>
<li>examining literature or interviewing people from similar community;</li>
<li>requesting information from provincial or national governments.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="hazard-description">Hazard description<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#hazard-description" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Five basic characteristics can be used to describe most hazards:</p>
<ul>
<li>intensity (how big, fast, and powerful);</li>
<li>frequency (the likelihood of a hazard causing an event of a given magnitude);</li>
<li>extent (the area that a hazard may affect);</li>
<li>time frame (warning time, duration, time of day, week, year);</li>
<li>manageability (whether anything can be done about it).</li>
</ul>
<p>KMTC</p>
<p>ISO 9001:2015 Certified</p>
<p>Downloaded by Birosoft Enterprises (<a href="mailto:birosoftenterprises@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">birosoftenterprises@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>For each hazard, these characteristics may mean different things. In a cyclone, for example, intensity might relate to wind speed, whereas in an earthquake, intensity relates to the number and strength of earth tremors. The example in the following section deals with flooding.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="description-of-a-flood-hazard">Description of a flood hazard<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#description-of-a-flood-hazard" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="flood-intensity">Flood intensity<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#flood-intensity" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Flood intensity may be described by height, class, depth, flow rate, and speed.</p>
<p><strong>Height.</strong> Flood height is often described in relation to a fixed marker, such as a post with heights marked on it, often placed in an arbitrary position near the river. Thus, a river height of 4 metres at one point on the river may be a fairly normal height, whereas the same height at a different place will indicate that the river is in flood.</p>
<p><strong>Class.</strong> Floods may also be described in terms of classes.</p>
<p>Definitions will vary from country to country, but the following are typical:</p>
<ul>
<li>Minor flooding  flooding that causes inconveniences, such as the closure of minor roads and the submergence of low-level bridges.</li>
<li>Moderate flooding  low-lying areas are inundated, requiring the removal of livestock and evacuation of some houses; main traffic bridges may also be submerged.</li>
<li>Major flooding  extensive rural areas are flooded, with properties and towns isolated; large urban areas are also flooded.</li>
</ul>
<p>In flood warnings, both the heights and the flood classes are often given for different points on a river; local people who receive the warnings may use their prior experience of either description to decide how to act.</p>
<p><strong>Depth.</strong> Another way of describing the intensity of floods is to relate flood heights to the floor levels of buildings that may be affected. This gives an idea of depth and is very useful for planning evacuation, land use, and building protection.</p>
<p><strong>Flow rate.</strong> Flow rate describes the volume of water flowing past a particular point in a given time period, and the sections are either cusecs (cubic feet per second) or cumecs (cubic metres per second), where 1 cusec _ 0.028 cumecs, and 35</p>
<p>cusecs _ 1 cumec.</p>
<p>This method of description is often used in relation to dam safety, as a very high flow rate over a dam with insufficient spillway capacity may lead to dam failure.</p>
<p><strong>Speed.</strong> Flood intensity may also be described in terms of the speed of the water at a given point. This is a useful measure since speed, coupled with water depth, will indicate the scale of damage of which the moving water is capable.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="description-of-technological-hazards">Description of technological hazards<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#description-of-technological-hazards" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Technological hazards are caused by the processes and materials of life in an industrialized world.</p>
<p>They include:</p>
<ul>
<li>the transport of people and materials (by road, rail, air, or sea);</li>
<li>the use of heavy or fast-moving machinery;</li>
<li>the use of high pressure, high temperature, electricity, etc;</li>
<li>the manufacture, storage, use, and disposal of hazardous materials.</li>
</ul>
<p>KMTC</p>
<p>ISO 9001:2015 Certified</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>39</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Birosoft Enterprises (<a href="mailto:birosoftenterprises@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">birosoftenterprises@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>The reasons for performing industrial hazard analysis depend on the perspective of those involved, and are summarized in the following paragraphs.</p>
<p>The community, including some members of the government, expects industry to be completely "safe". Attitudes towards the safety of industry tend to differ from attitudes towards safety in other activities, such as driving and sports, and are often unrealistic. Often, certain industries are targeted, while other less safe ones are ignored. For example, the degree of concern about industries involved in radioactive-related activities may be disproportionate compared with concern about other types of industries.</p>
<p>Industries are under increasing pressure from the community and government to minimize the risk of employee accidents and larger hazardous events that could affect the community. One measure that allows them to appear responsible is to perform hazard analysis.</p>
<p>Hazard analysis, coupled with comprehensive safety systems, has the potential to increase the viability of industry. Those industries that are aware of the principles of risk management may have differing degrees of concern about the risks involved in their activities, and may see sufficient benefit from the risks posed to allow them to continue at their current levels, or may consider the cost of reducing the risks to be too high. Industries that are unaware of the degree of risk involved in their activities or of the principles of risk management would probably resist any expenditure on hazard analysis or comprehensive safety systems.</p>
<p>A wide variation in the degree of safety of different types of industry, and in the interest that industrial management would have in performing hazard analysis, may therefore be expected.</p>
<p>Various government agencies may have the following involvement:</p>
<ul>
<li>ensuring that industry poses little threat to public safety from fire, explosion, or toxic emissions;</li>
<li>ensuring that workers' safety is within acceptable limits, with regard largely to minor accidents and injury and chronic toxic effects;</li>
<li>ensuring that public health is not affected adversely by chronic and acute toxic effects of industry;</li>
<li>ensuring that damage to the environment (including people) in the form of dust, smoke, noise, odours, gas, and liquid pollutants is minimized;</li>
<li>ensuring that land-use proposals (concerned with large areas rather than specific industries) involving hazardous industry zoning are appropriate;</li>
<li>ensuring that emergency planning is appropriate for hazardous industry and surrounding areas.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="manageability">Manageability<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#manageability" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The manageability of an industrial hazard for emergency preparedness purposes indicates what can be done about the hazard in terms of planning, training, and education, and carrying out drills. Regarding prevention, manageability of industrial hazards can be described in terms of the following types of controls, often called the "hierarchy of control":</p>
<ul>
<li>elimination (process or material);</li>
<li>replacement (process or material);</li>
<li>reduction (quantity of material, pressure, temperature, etc.);</li>
</ul>
<p>KMTC</p>
<p>ISO 9001:2015 Certified</p>
<p>Downloaded by Birosoft Enterprises (<a href="mailto:birosoftenterprises@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">birosoftenterprises@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>engineering control (over process or material);</li>
<li>separation from people, property, or environment;</li>
<li>administrative control;</li>
<li>emergency procedures;</li>
<li>personal protection.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="describing-the-community">Describing the community<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#describing-the-community" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="why-describe-the-community">Why describe the community?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#why-describe-the-community" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>The purpose of vulnerability assessment is to describe the interaction between hazards, the community, and the environment in order to develop programmes and strategies for protecting the community and the environment. Without knowledge of the community and environment, it is impossible to describe their vulnerability.</p>
<p>The characteristics shown in Table 13 are among those that can be used to describe a community.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="demography">Demography<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#demography" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Demography is the study of the statistics of human populations. Of the large quantities of data often available on the population of any given community, only</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="some-community-characteristics">Some community characteristics<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#some-community-characteristics" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Demography, Culture, Economy. Infrastructure and Environment</li>
<li>Population and age Traditions Trade Communication Landforms distribution</li>
<li>Ethnicity Agriculture/ networks Geology</li>
<li>Mobility Social values livestock Transportation Waterways</li>
<li>Useful skills Religion Investments networks Climate</li>
<li>Hazard awareness Attitudes to Industries Essential services Flora and fauna</li>
<li>Vulnerable groups hazards Wealth Community assets</li>
<li>Health level Normal food Government</li>
<li>Education level types structures</li>
<li>Sex distribution Eating habits Resource base</li>
<li>Power structures</li>
</ul>
<p>some characteristics are relevant to emergency management. These concern the number of people in the area of study, their distribution across the area, and any concentrations of vulnerable groups. Such groups may be vulnerable because of age (young or old), mobility (availability of transport), or disabilities. However, most people  not just these easily defined groups  are vulnerable to emergencies to some extent.</p>
<p>The following indicators are important as regards the communitys capacity for response and recovery:</p>
<p>KMTC</p>
<p>ISO 9001:2015 Certified 41</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Birosoft Enterprises (<a href="mailto:birosoftenterprises@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">birosoftenterprises@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Health indicators, which determine how much resistance people can offer to the health effects of an emergency; for example:</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>infant mortality rate indicates the health service coverage;</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>vaccination coverage rate indicates the extent and effectiveness of preventive programmes;</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>disease pattern indicates potential outbreaks of new disease or worsening of existing disease after an emergency;</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>malnutrition rate indicates how quickly and for how long feeding programmes may be needed.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Educational indicators, which determine how sophisticated the role of the community can be in participating in response activities and the level and type of public message that can be used; for example:</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>literacy rate, which is important for assessing the level of community participation and response that can be planned for;</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>female literacy rate, which is important for the success of health education and public preparedness.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>The best way to obtain demographic data on a community is to contact the government organization responsible. Data may be available in printed form or as computer files.</p>
<p>Another aspect of vulnerability is the ability of the community to manage hazards. Those who have a realistic perception of the hazards around them and are aware of the measures necessary to manage those hazards are better able to cope with emergencies. Certain community will have particular skills that are useful in emergency management. For example, a mining community would probably be better able to cope following storm damage or an earthquake than urban dwellers, owing to the available technical skills, and rural community would be more resilient than urban community because of their greater self sufficiency in normal times.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="culture">Culture<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#culture" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A community's culture, including its traditions, ethnicity, and social values, is highly relevant to emergency management. Attitudes towards hazards and vulnerability will be strongly influenced by attitudes towards nature, technology, the causation of accidents and emergencies, and the value of mitigating or contingent actions. Some community, for example, accept that lives will inevitably be lost in emergencies and may be unwilling to take preventive, preparatory, or response actions.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="economy">Economy<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#economy" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The economy of the community requires protection, and the more sensitive and vulnerable sections of the economy require careful consideration in emergency management.</p>
<p>It is likely that an emergency that causes considerable structural and environmental damage would devastate the local tourism industry, for example. Investment may also suffer because potential or current investors would regard the risks in the area as too high. Industries and trade might also suffer if disruption to transport and communications were to restrict access to goods and markets. Thus, the wealth of a community may also determine its resilience or its likelihood of sustaining harm.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="infrastructure">Infrastructure<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#infrastructure" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>KMTC</p>
<p>ISO 9001:2015 Certified 42</p>
<p>Downloaded by Birosoft Enterprises (<a href="mailto:birosoftenterprises@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">birosoftenterprises@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>The infrastructure (both physical and organizational) of a community is often highly vulnerable to hazards, particularly natural hazards. A vulnerability assessment should consider any possible damage to power generation and distribution systems, water supplies, communications systems, etc. These are often referred to as "lifelines", and relevant considerations include:</p>
<ul>
<li>effect of loss of services on the community;</li>
<li>possible extent of the damage;</li>
<li>alternative means of supplying the service;</li>
<li>time required for repairs;</li>
<li>cost of repairs.</li>
</ul>
<p>It is also important to have a basic description of the government structure, and of service and community organizations, since they will provide the mechanism for emergency management programmes and strategies.</p>
<p>Any other characteristics of a community that are relevant to emergency management should also be considered.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="environment">Environment<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#environment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The environment is an important determinant of settlement patterns and lifestyles of community; it can be defined as the natural surroundings, including plants and animals, water, air, and soil. Damage to any of these elements may affect other elements of the environment. Many hazards can adversely affect the environment, including chronic (continuous and low-level) or acute (sudden and high-level) pollution by hazardous materials.</p>
<p>Paradoxically, while the environment nurtures the community, it can also be the source of some of the greatest natural hazards. Describing the environment in a vulnerability assessment will often identify some hazards that have not yet been considered.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="community-and-environment-mapping">Community and environment mapping<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#community-and-environment-mapping" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>As with hazards, detailed information about a community can be documented effectively with maps.</p>
<p>This is particularly true when the characteristics that describe the community vary systematically over a geographical area.</p>
<p>The community information that can be mapped includes:</p>
<ul>
<li>Population density</li>
<li>Proposed food distribution points</li>
<li>Particularly vulnerable groups </li>
<li>Water and sanitation information</li>
<li>prisons,</li>
<li>mental hospitals,</li>
<li>Health centres</li>
<li>orphanages, homes for the</li>
<li>Warehouses</li>
<li>disabled, and new and unplanned</li>
<li>Utility networks and distribution settlements</li>
<li>points  electricity, gas, water</li>
<li>Potential emergency shelter</li>
<li>Communication networks sites</li>
</ul>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="description-of-effects-and-vulnerability">Description of effects and vulnerability<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#description-of-effects-and-vulnerability" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="how-are-effects-and-vulnerability-described">How are effects and vulnerability described?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#how-are-effects-and-vulnerability-described" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>The way in which vulnerability and the effects of hazards are described will depend on the scope of the vulnerability assessment. If a community is assessed, a standard set of parameters to describe the effects (e.g. extent and number of services disrupted, number of homeless persons) can be used. For a hospital, however, other parameters (e.g. effect of loss of service on the community, emergency medical demands on the hospital, effects on staff, and cost of and time required for repairs) would be useful. Table 14 shows some possible parameters for describing community vulnerability and the effects of hazards on a community.</p>
<p>These possible parameters should be discussed with the planning group and modified if necessary. Each hazard should then be examined in detail, parameter by parameter, to estimate the degree of loss in relation to each parameter in the community. The differential vulnerability of parts of the community in respect of these parameters can also be described, and the results of the entire examination should be documented immediately. The planning group should also realize that one emergency may provoke others. There is usually, in fact, a cascade effect, more and different emergencies following the original. These, too should be planned for. There are also specific needs that can be predicted for different types of emergencies.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="in-addition">In addition:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#in-addition" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<ul>
<li>Volcanic eruptions. Possible needs (and secondary effects) are similar to those for earthquakes within the area directly affected by the eruption; there may be population displacements.</li>
<li>Tsunamis (tidal waves caused by earthquakes). Possible needs are similar to those of tropical storms plus floods, with the added complication of contamination of wells and agricultural land by salt water.</li>
<li>Epidemics. Needs usually include specific drugs, transport, surveillance, improvement of water supplies, personal hygiene and sanitation; reinforcement of health service management may also be required.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="emergency-planning">Emergency planning<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#emergency-planning" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="introduction">Introduction<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#introduction" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="what-is-an-emergency-plan">What is an emergency plan?<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#what-is-an-emergency-plan" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>An emergency plan is an agreed set of arrangements for responding to and recovering from emergencies; it describes responsibilities, management structures, strategies, and resources.</p>
<p>KMTC
ISO 9001:2015 Certified
Downloaded by Birosoft Enterprises (<a href="mailto:birosoftenterprises@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">birosoftenterprises@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Why develop plans?</p>
<p>People who do not believe planning is necessary argue that:</p>
<ul>
<li>everybody knows what to do;</li>
<li>emergencies are unpredictable and impossible to plan for;</li>
<li>people do not follow plans in emergencies;</li>
<li>developing emergency plans will worry the public.</li>
</ul>
<p>COMPREHENSIVE DISASTER PREPAREDNESS STRATEGY WOULD THEREFORE INCLUDE THE FOLLOWING ELEMENTS</p>
<table><thead><tr><th>1. Hazard, risk and Vulnerability assessments</th><th>2. Response mechanisms and strategies</th><th>3. Preparedness plans</th></tr></thead><tbody><tr><td>4. Coordination</td><td>5. Information management</td><td>6. Early warning systems</td></tr><tr><td>7. Resource mobilization</td><td>8. Public education, training, &amp; rehearsals</td><td>9. Community-Based disaster preparedness</td></tr></tbody></table>
<ol>
<li>Hazard, risk and vulnerability assessments</li>
<li>Response mechanisms and strategies</li>
<li>Preparedness plans</li>
<li>Coordination</li>
<li>Information management</li>
<li>Early warning systems</li>
<li>Resource mobilization</li>
<li>Public education, training, &amp; rehearsals</li>
<li>Community-Based disaster preparedness</li>
</ol>
<p>The ninth element, "Community based disaster preparedness" (CBDP), should not be seen as a measure distinct from the other elements. Rather, CBDP is a process that encompasses and incorporates the first eight elements into a locally appropriate and locally "owned" strategy for disaster preparedness and risk reduction. This section will discuss aspects of the first eight elements. Section 5 provides the rationale for and examples of community based disaster preparedness.</p>
<p>Hazard, risk and vulnerability assessments</p>
<p>All planning and implementation of disaster preparedness measures should be based on an assessment and prioritization of the hazards and risks that people face, as well as their ability or inability to cope with and withstand the effects of those hazards. This assessment should:</p>
<ul>
<li>Identify the characteristics, frequency and potential severity of the hazards a community faces</li>
</ul>
<p>KMTC
ISO 9001:2015 Certified 45</p>
<p>This document is available on
studocu
Downloaded by Birosoft Enterprises (<a href="mailto:birosoftenterprises@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">birosoftenterprises@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>&gt; Identify the particular geographical areas and community that are most susceptible and vulnerable to those hazards
&gt; Identify the main sectors of a community (population, infrastructure, housing, services, etc.) that would be affected by a specific type of hazard and anticipate how they might be affected
&gt; Assess the ability of those sectors to withstand and cope with the effects of hazardous phenomena</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="response-mechanisms-and-strategies">Response mechanisms and strategies<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#response-mechanisms-and-strategies" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>There are many preparedness mechanisms and strategies that will strengthen and increase the effectiveness of an emergency response. These include development or formation of:</p>
<p>i. evacuation procedures (including how to disseminate these procedures to the public)
ii. search and rescue teams (including plans for training them)
iii. assessment teams (including plans for training them)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="phases-iii-and-iv-response-and-recovery">Phases III and IV: Response and Recovery<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#phases-iii-and-iv-response-and-recovery" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Disaster response is the sum total of actions taken by people and institutions in the face of disaster. These actions commence with the warning of an oncoming threatening event or with the event itself if it occurs without warning. The focus in the response and recovery phases of the</p>
<p>KMTC</p>
<p>ISO 9001:2015 Certified 46</p>
<p>Downloaded by Birosoft Enterprises (<a href="mailto:birosoftenterprises@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">birosoftenterprises@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>disaster management cycle is on meeting the basic needs of the people until more permanent and sustainable solutions can be found.</p>
<p>Developmental considerations contribute to all aspects of the disaster management cycle. One of the main goals of disaster management, and one of its strongest links with development, is the promotion of sustainable livelihoods and their protection and recovery during disasters and emergencies. Where this goal is achieved, people have a greater capacity to deal with disasters and their recovery is more rapid and long lasting. In a development oriented disaster management approach, the objectives are to reduce hazards, prevent disasters, and prepare for emergencies.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="upon-completion-of-this-section-you-will-be-able-to">Upon completion of this section you will be able to:<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#upon-completion-of-this-section-you-will-be-able-to" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="outcomes">Outcomes<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#outcomes" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="terminology">Terminology<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#terminology" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p><strong>Development</strong>: A step or stage in growth or advancement in society, economics or in politics for a better lifestyle.</p>
<p><strong>Evacuation</strong>: Removal from hazardous place to another that is safe.</p>
<p><strong>Humanitarian</strong>: The act of promoting the welfare of humanity, especially through the elimination of pain and suffering.</p>
<p><strong>Logistics</strong>: The branch of civil defence or agency that have to do with procuring, maintaining, and transporting materiel, personnel, and facilities.</p>
<p><strong>Recovery</strong>: The return of buildings and infrastructure to a normal or improved state after a setback or loss.</p>
<p><strong>Relief</strong>: Private or public help in the form of money, food, clothing, shelter, or medicine, provided to people who are temporarily suffering from the effects of disaster and are at the time completely helpless.</p>
<p><strong>Remittance</strong>: Sending of money to pay for resources or services to help people in need after a disaster.</p>
<p><strong>Reconstruction</strong>: A community or structure that has been reorganized, reformed, or restored after being impacted by a disaster or other hazard.</p>
<p><strong>Rehabilitation</strong>: To restore buildings or parts of towns, to their former condition or better.</p>
<p><strong>Response</strong>: Actions taken in reaction to a disaster or similar hazards.</p>
<p><strong>Security</strong>: Safety measures that provide a sense of protection against loss or harm from disaster or uncertain circumstances.</p>
<p><strong>Volunteerism</strong>: The practice of using volunteer workers, especially in community service or disaster organizations and programmes.</p>
<p><strong>Warning</strong>: Advice given to somebody or persons to be careful of impending danger.</p>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="disaster-response">Disaster Response<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#disaster-response" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>The aim of emergency response is to provide immediate assistance to maintain life, improve health, and to support the morale of the affected population. Such assistance may range from providing specific but limited aid, such as assisting refugees with transportation, temporary shelter, and food, to establishing semi-permanent settlement in camps and other locations. It also may involve initial repairs to damaged infrastructure.</p>
<p>The focus in the response phase is on meeting the basic needs of the people until more permanent and sustainable solutions can be found.</p>
<p>Humanitarian organizations are often strongly present in this phase of the disaster management cycle.</p>
<p>During a disaster, humanitarian agencies are often called upon to deal with immediate response and recovery.</p>
<p>To be able to respond effectively, these agencies must have experienced leaders, trained personnel, adequate transportation and logistic support, appropriate communications, and guidelines for working in emergencies. If the necessary preparations have not been made, the humanitarian agencies will not be able to meet the immediate needs of the people.</p>
<p>This section identifies the principal activities of disaster response. Each activity is (formally or informally) governed by a set of policies and procedures, typically under the auspices of a lead agency. In the end, disaster response activities are implemented by multiple government organizations, international and national agencies, local entities and individuals, each with their roles and responsibilities.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="aims-of-disaster-response">Aims of disaster response<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#aims-of-disaster-response" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>i. To ensure the survival of the maximum possible number of victims, keeping them in the best possible health in the circumstances.
ii. To re-establish self-sufficiency and essential services as quickly as possible for all population groups, with special attention to those whose needs are greatest: the most vulnerable and underprivileged.
iii. To repair or replace damaged infrastructure and regenerate viable economic activities. To do this in a manner that contributes to long term development goals and reduces vulnerability to any future recurrence of potentially damaging hazards.
iv. In situations of civil or international conflict, the aim is to protect and assist the civilian population, in close collaboration with the International Committee of the Red Cross (ICRC) and in compliance with international conventions.
v. In cases involving population displacements (due to any type of disaster) the aim is to find durable solutions as quickly as possible, while ensuring protection and assistance as necessary in the meantime.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="disaster-response-activities">Disaster Response Activities<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#disaster-response-activities" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The following are typical activities of emergency response:</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="1-warning">1 Warning<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-warning" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Warning refers to information concerning the nature of the danger and imminent disaster threats. Warnings must be rapidly disseminated to government officials, institutions and the population at large in the areas at immediate risk so that appropriate actions may be taken, namely, either to</p>
<p>KMTC</p>
<p>ISO 9001:2015 Certified 48</p>
<p>Downloaded by Birosoft Enterprises (<a href="mailto:birosoftenterprises@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">birosoftenterprises@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>evacuate or secure property and prevent further damage. The warning could be disseminated via radio, television, the written press, telephone system and cell phone.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="2-evacuation-and-migration">2 Evacuation and migration<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-evacuation-and-migration" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Evacuation involves the relocation of a population from zones at risk of an imminent disaster to a safer location. The primary concern is the protection of life of the community and immediate treatment of those who may be injured.</p>
<p>Evacuation is most commonly associated with tropical storms but is also a frequent requirement with technological or industrial hazards. For evacuation to work there must be:</p>
<ul>
<li>A timely and accurate warning system,</li>
<li>Clear identification of escape routes,</li>
<li>An established policy that requires everyone to evacuate when an order is given,</li>
<li>A public education programme to make the community aware of the plan.</li>
</ul>
<p>In the case of a slow onset of a disaster, for example severe drought, the movement of people from the zone where they are at risk to a safer site is not, in fact, evacuation, but crisis-induced migration. This movement is usually not organized and coordinated by authorities but is a spontaneous response to the perception by the migrants that food and/or security can be obtained elsewhere.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="3-search-and-rescue-sar">3 Search and rescue (SAR)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#3-search-and-rescue-sar" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Search and rescue (SAR) is the process of identifying the location of disaster victims that may be trapped or isolated and bringing them to safety and medical attention. In the aftermath of tropical storms and floods, SAR usually includes locating stranded flood victims, who may be threatened by rising water, and either bringing them to safety or providing them with food and first aid until they can be evacuated or returned to their homes. In the aftermath of earthquakes, SAR normally focuses on locating people who are trapped and/ or injured in collapsed buildings.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="4-post-disaster-assessment">4 Post-disaster assessment<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#4-post-disaster-assessment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The primary objective of assessment is to provide a clear, concise picture of the post-disaster situation, to identify relief needs and to develop strategies for recovery. It determines options for humanitarian assistance, how best to utilize existing resources, or to develop requests for further assistance.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="5-response-and-relief">5 Response and relief<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#5-response-and-relief" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>When a disaster has occurred response and relief have to take place immediately; there can be no delays. It is therefore important to have contingency plans in place. Relief is the provision on a humanitarian basis of material aid and emergency medical care necessary to save and preserve human lives. It also enables families to meet their basic needs for medical and health care, shelter, clothing, water, and food (including the means to prepare food).</p>
<p>Relief supplies or services are typically provided, free of charge, in the days and weeks immediately following a sudden disaster. In the case of deteriorating slow-onset emergency situations and population displacements (refugees, internally and externally displaced people), emergency relief may be needed for extended periods.</p>
