<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="introduction-to-public-health-emergency-preparedness-and-response">Introduction to public health emergency preparedness and response<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#introduction-to-public-health-emergency-preparedness-and-response" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>There is no country that is immune from disasters or hazards, though vulnerability varies. Disasters cannot be predicted. When they happen, they create an emergency situation and impact on the health care system through increased demand for essential services, such as the provision of health care or destruction.</p>
<p>For this purpose, health care professionals should understand, be prepared for, and participate in preparing for, responding to, and recovering from the impacts of disasters.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="definitions-and-concepts-of-disaster-management">Definitions and Concepts of Disaster Management<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#definitions-and-concepts-of-disaster-management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="crisis">Crisis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#crisis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>An event or series of events representing a critical threat to the health, safety, security or wellbeing of a community, usually over a wide area. Armed conflicts, epidemics, famine, natural disasters, environmental emergencies and other major harmful events may involve or lead to a humanitarian crisis.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="emergency">Emergency<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#emergency" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is a situation generated by the real or imminent occurrence of an event that requires immediate attention. Paying immediate attention to an event or situation is important as the event/situation can generate negative consequences and escalate into an emergency. The purpose of emergency planning is to minimize those consequences.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="emergency-preparedness">Emergency preparedness<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#emergency-preparedness" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>A program me of long-term activities whose goals are to strengthen the overall capacity and capability of a country or a community to manage efficiently all types of emergencies and bring about an orderly transition from relief through recovery and back to normalcy. It requires that emergency plans be in place, personnel at all levels and in all</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>3</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>sectors be trained, and communities at risk be educated, and that these measures be monitored and evaluated regularly.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="hazard">Hazard<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#hazard" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This is the potential for a natural or human-caused event to occur with negative consequences. A hazard can become an emergency, when the emergency moves beyond the control of the population, it becomes a disaster.</p>
<p>i. Natural hazard</p>
<p>Natural processes or phenomenon that may cause loss of life, injury or other health impacts, property damage, loss of livelihoods and services, social and economic disruption, or environmental damage.</p>
<p>ii. Geological hazard</p>
<p>Geological process or phenomenon that may cause loss of life, injury or other health impacts, property damage, loss of livelihoods and services, social and economic disruption, or environmental damage.</p>
<p>iii. Technological hazard</p>
<p>A hazard originating from technological or industrial conditions, including accidents, dangerous procedures, infrastructure failures or specific human activities, that may cause loss of life, injury, illness or other health impacts, property damage, loss of livelihoods and services, social and economic disruption, or environmental damage.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="disaster">Disaster<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#disaster" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>This a natural or human-caused event which causes intensive negative impacts on people, goods, services and/or the environment, exceeding the affected community's capability to respond.</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>i. Disaster risk</p>
<p>The potential disaster losses, in lives, health status, livelihoods, assets and services, which could occur to a particular community or a society over some specified future time period.</p>
<p>ii. Disaster risk management</p>
<p>The systematic process of using administrative directives, organizations, and operational skills and capacities to implement strategies, policies and improved coping capacities in order to lessen the adverse impacts of hazards and the possibility of disaster.</p>
<p>iii. Disaster risk reduction</p>
<p>The concept and practice of reducing disaster risks through systematic efforts to analyses and manage the causal factors of disasters, including through reduced exposure to hazards, lessened vulnerability of people and property, wise management of land and the environment, and improved preparedness for adverse events.</p>
<p>Risk</p>
<p>This is the probability that loss will occur as the result of an adverse event, given the hazard and the vulnerability. Risk (R) can be determined as a product of hazard (H) and vulnerability (V). i.e. R = H x V.</p>
<p>The probability of harmful consequences or expected losses such as deaths, injuries, property, disrupted livelihood and economic activity, or environment damaged resulting from interactions between natural or human-induced hazards and vulnerabilities.</p>
<p>i. Risk assessment</p>
<p>A methodology to determine the nature and extent of risk by analyzing potential hazards and evaluating existing conditions of vulnerability that together could potentially harm exposed people, property, services, livelihoods and the environment on which they depend.</p>
<p>ii. Risk management</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>5</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Consists of identifying threats (hazards likely to occur), determining their probability of occurrence, estimating what the impact of the threat might be to the communities at risk, determining measures that can reduce the risk, and taking action to reduce the threat.</p>
<p>iii. Risk reduction</p>
<p>Taking measures to either prevent hazards from creating risks or to lessen the distribution, intensity or severity of hazards. These measures include awareness raising and improving public health security.</p>
<p>Vulnerability</p>
<p>The degree to which a population or an individual is unable to anticipate, cope with, resist and recover from the impact of a disaster.</p>
<p>Types of Vulnerability and their Characteristics</p>
<ol>
<li>Tangible/Material (easy to see; value easily determined);</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>People  lives, health, security, living conditions</li>
<li>Property  services, physical property loss, loss of use</li>
<li>Economy  loss of products and production, income</li>
<li>Environment  water, soil, air, vegetation, wildlife</li>
</ul>
<ol start="2">
<li>Intangible/Abstract (difficult to see; value difficult to determine)</li>
</ol>
<ul>
<li>Social structures  family and community relationships</li>
<li>Cultural practices  religious and agricultural</li>
<li>Cohesion  disruption of normal life</li>
<li>Motivation  will to recover; government response</li>
</ul>
<p>Factors Contributing to Vulnerability</p>
<ol>
<li>Poverty; People who are already in a depressed state are less able to recover. Some people are even more vulnerable  pregnant women, children and the disabled.</li>
<li>Population growth; Population has grown dramatically over the past decade</li>
<li>Rapid urbanization; Growing concentration around the capital. For example, two-thirds of the Nairobi population lives in slums.</li>
</ol>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ol start="4">
<li>Transition in cultural practices; Increase in sub-standard housing in more heavily populated urban areas.</li>
<li>Changes in traditional coping mechanisms; Declines in self-reliance, food conservation and preservation, warning systems etc.</li>
<li>Environmental degradation; as resources are consumed, vegetation cover removed, water polluted and air fouled, a country is more vulnerable to a disaster.</li>
<li>Lack of awareness and information; when people and government officials are unaware or lack information about disaster management, they fail to take appropriate actions.</li>
<li>Civil Strife and unrest; Resources are consumed, people are in a stressed situation, and transportation is restricted.</li>
<li>Geographical isolation; Island countries are disadvantaged by their relative remoteness, particular their limited access to schools, health and cash.</li>
<li>High disaster impact; Limited economies (tourism, agriculture). Disaster impact can affect an entire economy.</li>
<li>Political uncertainties/instability; Changing government policies, changing personnel in the national focal point, economic weakness all can contribute to an effective national disaster management program.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="distinguishing-between-an-emergency-and-a-disaster-situation">Distinguishing Between an Emergency and a Disaster Situation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#distinguishing-between-an-emergency-and-a-disaster-situation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>An emergency and a disaster are two different situations:</p>
<ul>
<li>An emergency is a situation in which the community is capable of coping. It is a situation generated by the real or imminent occurrence of an event that requires immediate attention of emergency resources;</li>
<li>A disaster is a situation in which the community is incapable of coping. It is a natural or human-caused event which causes intense negative impacts on people, goods, services and/or the environment, exceeding the affected community's capability to respond. Therefore the community seeks the assistance of government and international agencies.</li>
</ul>
<p>This document is available on
5 studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="classification-of-disasters">Classification of disasters<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#classification-of-disasters" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Types of natural and non-natural disasters</p>
<p>Disasters are often classified according to their:</p>
<ol>
<li>Causes  natural vs. human</li>
<li>Speed of onset  sudden vs. slow</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="a-causes-natural-vs-human">A. CAUSES (NATURAL VS. HUMAN)<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#a-causes-natural-vs-human" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="1-natural-disasters">1. Natural Disasters<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#1-natural-disasters" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>These types of disaster naturally occur in proximity to, and pose a threat to, people, structures or economic assets. They are caused by biological, geological, seismic, hydrologic, or meteorological conditions or processes in the natural environment (e.g., cyclones, earthquakes, tsunami, floods, landslides, and volcanic eruptions).</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="2-human-made-disasters">2. Human-Made Disasters<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#2-human-made-disasters" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>These are disasters or emergency situations of which the principal, direct causes are identifiable human actions, deliberate or otherwise. Apart from technological disasters this mainly involves situations in which civilian populations suffer casualties, losses of property, basic services and means of livelihood as a result of war, civil strife or other conflicts, or policy implementation. In many cases, people are forced to leave their homes, giving rise to congregations of refugees or externally and/or internally displaced persons as a result of civil strife, an airplane crash, a major fire, oil spill, epidemic, terrorism, etc.</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>B. SPEED OF ONSET</p>
<ol>
<li>
<p>Sudden onset: little or no warning, minimal time to prepare. For example, an earthquake, tsunami, cyclone, volcano, etc.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Slow onset: adverse event slow to develop; first the situation develops; the second level is an emergency; the third level is a disaster. For example, drought, civil strife, epidemic, etc.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>Generally, Common Disasters include</p>
<ul>
<li>Epidemic</li>
<li>Earthquake</li>
<li>Fire</li>
<li>Flood</li>
<li>Cyber attack</li>
<li>Sabotage (insider or external threat)</li>
<li>Hurricane or other major storm</li>
<li>Power outage</li>
<li>Water outage (supply interruption, contamination)</li>
<li>Telecoms outage</li>
<li>IT outage</li>
<li>Terrorism/Piracy</li>
<li>War/civil disorder</li>
<li>Theft (insider or external threat, vital information or material)</li>
<li>Random failure of mission-critical systems</li>
<li>Single point dependency</li>
</ul>

<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="implications-effects-of-disasters-on-the-community-and-environment">Implications / Effects of Disasters on the Community and Environment<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#implications-effects-of-disasters-on-the-community-and-environment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>A disaster in any region has a devastating impact. The following list identifies a few of the unpleasant impacts:</p>
<ul>
<li>Infrastructure damage</li>
<li>Telecommunication loss</li>
<li>Flooding</li>
<li>Landslides</li>
<li>Power disruption</li>
<li>Water problems</li>
<li>Agricultural damage</li>
<li>Loss/damage to housing</li>
<li>Damage to inland and coastal environments</li>
<li>Disruption of standard of living, lifestyle, etc.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="types-of-disasters-and-hazards">Types of Disasters and Hazards<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#types-of-disasters-and-hazards" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>There are four main types of disasters:</p>
<ol>
<li>Natural disasters- These include drought, floods, hurricanes, earthquakes and volcano eruptions that can have immediate impacts on human health as well as secondary impacts causing further death and suffering from floods causing landslides, earthquakes resulting in fires, tsunamis causing widespread flooding and typhoons sinking ferries.</li>
<li>Environmental emergencies- These include technological or industrial accidents usually involving hazardous material and occur where these materials are produced, used or transported. Forest fires are generally included in this definition because they tend to be caused by humans.</li>
</ol>
<p>10</p>
<ol start="3">
<li>
<p>Complex emergencies- These emergencies involve a break-down of authority, looting and attacks on strategic installations. Complex emergencies include conflict situations and war.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Epidemics and pandemic- These emergencies involve a sudden onset of a disease that raises well beyond the expected numbers that the health system can handle. An epidemic is specific to one city, region or country while a pandemic covers a much wider geographical area and goes much further than national borders often worldwide.</p>
</li>
</ol>
<p>A pandemic also infects many more people than an epidemic. These affect and disrupt health services and businesses leading to economic and social costs.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="human-induced-disasters">Human Induced Disasters<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#human-induced-disasters" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Some disasters are caused by human factors these include:</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="transport-related-accidents">Transport related accidents<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#transport-related-accidents" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The increasing numbers of accidents that take place during road, air and water, and railway transportation are occasionally fatal and hazardous. Currently, road accidents are more frequent because road transportation is used more often than air, railway and water transport. In addition, motorcycle (boda-boda) accidents have become notoriously rampart and fatal. Occasionally, there are reported incidents of boats that capsize. On some occasions, crocodiles attack people who use small canoes.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="fires">Fires<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#fires" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Fire hazards include the unplanned and massive burning which may cause destruction of equipment, settlements, property and life. Among the many factors that cause fire hazards are poor electric wiring, power outage, poor construction standards, accidents, arson and uncontrolled burning of bush or waste materials. Fires are most common in</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>8</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>industries and congested human settlements. Road transportation of oil, gas and petroleum resources which are highly inflammable increase the risk of fires in the event of road accidents followed by people trying to illegally siphon the products.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="terrorism">Terrorism<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#terrorism" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Terrorism is coordinated crime and brutal aggression against government and civilian establishments. The first major terrorist attack occurred in 1998 targeting the American Embassy. Since then, the country has been under a constant threat of terrorism.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="impact-of-disasters-on-the-health-system">Impact of Disasters on the Health System<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#impact-of-disasters-on-the-health-system" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>All forms of disasters and hazards affect human life. They impact on, and can at times compromise public health infrastructure and systems. What may first result in direct injuries and death may rapidly change to excess indirect illness and subsequent death as essential public health resources are destroyed, deteriorate, or are systematically denied to vulnerable populations.</p>
<p>The public health consequences include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Death</li>
<li>Injuries</li>
<li>Public concern for safety</li>
<li>Panic</li>
<li>Increases pests vectors</li>
<li>Worsening chronic illness</li>
<li>Loss of clean water</li>
<li>Loss of shelter</li>
<li>Loss of sanitation</li>
<li>Loss of routine hygiene</li>
<li>Damage to health care system</li>
<li>Toxic hazardous exposure</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="disaster-management-cycle">DISASTER MANAGEMENT CYCLE<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#disaster-management-cycle" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Disaster management is a cyclical process. The end of one phase is the beginning of another, although one phase of the cycle does not necessarily have to be completed in order for the next to take place. Often, several phases take place concurrently. Timely decision making during each phase results in greater preparedness, better warnings, reduced vulnerability and or the prevention of future disasters.</p>
<p>A complete disaster management cycle includes the shaping of public policies and plans that either addresses the causes of disasters or mitigates their effects on people, property, and infrastructure.</p>
<p>This document is available on
G
studocu
Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Figure 1. Disaster Management Cycle
<img src="assets/images/image-20260111-dc7d496d.jpeg" alt="img-10.jpeg">
Source; National Policy for Disaster Management in Kenya</p>
<p>Mitigation- Measures put in place to minimize the results from a disaster e.g. building codes and zoning, vulnerability analyses and public education.</p>
<p>Preparedness- Planning how to respond e.g. preparedness plans, emergency exercises/training and early warning systems.</p>
<p>Response- Initial actions taken as the event takes place. It involves efforts to minimize the hazards created by a disaster e.g. evacuation, search and rescue, and emergency relief.</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Recovery- Returning the community to normal. Ideally, the affected area should be put in a condition equal to or better than it was before the disaster took place e.g. temporary housing, financial support and medical care.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="disaster-mitigation">Disaster Mitigation<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#disaster-mitigation" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Mitigation refers to all actions taken before a disaster to reduce its impacts, including preparedness and long-term risk reduction measures.</p>
<p>Mitigation activities fall broadly into two categories:</p>
<ol>
<li>Structural mitigation  construction projects which reduce economic and social impacts</li>
<li>Non-structural activities  policies and practices which raise awareness of hazards or encourage developments to reduce the impact of disasters</li>
</ol>
<p>Mitigation includes reviewing building codes; vulnerability analysis updates; zoning and land-use management and planning; reviewing of building use regulations and safety codes; and implementing preventative health measures. (World Development Report, 1998)</p>
<p>Mitigation can also involve educating businesses and the public on simple measures they can take to reduce loss or injury, for instance fastening bookshelves, water heaters, and filing cabinets to walls to keep them from falling during earthquakes. Ideally, these preventative measures and public education programs will occur before the disaster.</p>
<p>The primary focus of disaster management is to prevent disasters wherever possible or to mitigate those which are inevitable.</p>
<p>Four sets of tools that could be used to prevent or mitigate disasters include:</p>
<p>i. Hazard management and vulnerability reduction
ii. Economic diversification
iii. Political intervention and commitment
iv. Public awareness</p>

<p>The first two apply exclusively to disasters caused by natural phenomena while the latter are used to mitigate any other hazards.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="mitigation-strategies">Mitigation strategies<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#mitigation-strategies" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Two aspects of mitigation include:</p>
<ol>
<li>Hazard identification and vulnerability analysis and</li>
<li>Various mitigation strategies or measures.</li>
</ol>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="mitigation-strategies-or-measures">Mitigation strategies or measures<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#mitigation-strategies-or-measures" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Adjusting normal development programs to reduce losses.</li>
</ul>
<p>For instance, varieties of crops that are more wind, flood or drought resistant can often be introduced in areas prone to floods, drought and cyclones.</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Economic diversification. In areas where the principal or sole source of the income may be threatened, attempts should be made to diversify the economy and introduce the economic activities that are less vulnerable. Diversification is extremely important where economies are dependent on a single cash crop.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Developing disaster resistant economic activities. Some economic activities are relatively unaffected by disasters. For instance, situating warehouses in flood plains may be more appropriate than manufacturing plants in the same location. Coconut palms could be more suitable than other fruit trees in cyclone-prone coastal areas.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<p>Efforts should be made to identify and encourage the development of enterprises that are less vulnerable to the hazards.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="objectives-of-disaster-management">Objectives of Disaster Management<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#objectives-of-disaster-management" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="i-reduce-damages-and-deaths">I. Reduce damages and deaths<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#i-reduce-damages-and-deaths" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Effective disaster management reduces or avoids morbidity, mortality, and economic and physical damages from a hazard. The methods used to achieve this include hazard and vulnerability analysis, preparedness, mitigation and prevention measures, and the use of predictive and warning systems.</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>ii. Reduce personal suffering</p>
<p>Disaster management reduces personal suffering such as morbidity and emotional stress following a hazard. The methods used to prevent suffering include hazard and vulnerability analysis, preparedness, and mitigation and prevention measures. Examples of efforts to reduce personal suffering include providing safe food supplies and potable drinking water when water supplies become contaminated.</p>
<p>iii. Speed recovery</p>
<p>The methods to accomplish this objective include effective response mechanisms and the institution of recovery programs and assistance. Examples of efforts to speed recovery include providing paperwork assistance for insurance claims, and grant or loan applications.</p>
<p>iv. Protect victims</p>
<p>Disaster management provides protection to victims and or displaced persons. Facilities utilize preparedness, response mechanisms, recovery programs and assistance to address shelter needs and provide protective services.</p>
<p>Disaster Risk Management Plan</p>
<p>An effective disaster risk management plan includes:</p>
<p>i. Prevention</p>
<p>Prevention includes activities designed to provide permanent protection from disaster. Disaster prevention plans are designed to provide permanent protection from disasters. Not all disasters, particularly natural disasters can be prevented, but the risk of loss of life and injury can be mitigated with good evacuation plans, environmental planning and design standards.</p>
<p>ii. Preparedness</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>8</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>These are activities designed to minimize loss of life and damage e.g. by removing people and property from a threatened location and by facilitating timely and effective rescue, relief and rehabilitation. Preparedness is the main way of reducing the impact of disasters. Community based preparedness and management should be a high priority in health systems management. Appropriate preparedness results in persons knowing what to do and how to respond after disaster has occurred.</p>
<p>iii. Mitigation</p>
<p>This is a coordinated multi-sectorial response to reduce the impact of a disaster and its long-term results. Relief activities include rescue, relocation, providing food and water, preventing disease and disability, repairing vital services such as telecommunications and transport, providing temporary shelter and emergency health care.</p>
<p>iv. Recovery</p>
<p>Once emergency needs have been met and the initial crisis is over, the people affected and the communities that support them are still vulnerable. Recovery activities include rebuilding infrastructure, health care and rehabilitation. These should blend with development activities, such as building human resources for health and developing policies and practices to avoid similar situations in future.</p>
<p>Hazard identification</p>
<p>Hazard identification determines the hazards that may affect people in a community. This third step in the vulnerability assessment process provides information for further analysis. Hazard identification is not straightforward  people may have quite different perceptions of what constitutes a significant hazard. For this reason seeking the views of a number of people in the community is essential.</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="a-group-technique-for-identifying-hazards">A group technique for identifying hazards<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#a-group-technique-for-identifying-hazards" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Hazard identification should be undertaken by a group of people, such as a planning group, with expertise in the area of work and a commitment to the safety of that area. One quick method to determine peoples perceptions of the most serious hazards and avoid the pitfalls of groupthink, is the following:</p>
<ul>
<li>Each person in the group should be asked to write down the 10 hazards (in the area being investigated) that most concern them, and be given a few minutes to do this.</li>
<li>When they have finished the first task, they should rank, in terms of seriousness, the hazards they have listed as high, medium, and low (using their own definition of seriousness).</li>
<li>Each person should then say what he or she has written down (without the ranking) and answers should be recorded on a blackboard, whiteboard, or large sheet of paper. Duplications should not be recorded; if very similar hazards are mentioned, planning group members should refine what they mean. Suggestions must not be belittled, but recorded uncritically.</li>
<li>Group members should be asked about each hazard listed; the numbers of people who consider each hazard to be high, medium, or low in seriousness should be recorded.</li>
</ul>
<p>This technique has the following benefits.</p>
<ul>
<li>It allows everyone to have their say and avoids some of the problems of groupthink. If everyone is allowed to contribute, the likelihood of developing a meaningful vulnerability assessment is greater.</li>
<li>It encourages interaction between people who may not know each other and may encourage all group members to continue contributing.</li>
<li>It prompts the members of the planning group to think analytically.</li>
<li>It demonstrates to all members of the group that people have divergent points of view concerning hazard and risk and will to some extent validate these different points of view.</li>
<li>It increases members commitment to the vulnerability assessment because they have had a chance to contribute.</li>
</ul>
<p>Other techniques for identifying hazards include:</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>8</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>a. Researching The History Of Emergencies In The Community, By Consulting Histories, Newspapers, Records, And Older Community Members;
b. Inspecting The Community For Evidence Of Previous Emergencies, Existing Hazards, And Existing Vulnerability;
c. Examining Literature Or Interviewing People From Similar Community;
d. Requesting Information From Provincial Or National Governments.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="hazard-description">HAZARD DESCRIPTION<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#hazard-description" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Five basic characteristics can be used to describe most hazards:</p>
<ul>
<li>intensity (how big, fast, and powerful);</li>
<li>frequency (the likelihood of a hazard causing an event of a given magnitude);</li>
<li>extent (the area that a hazard may affect);</li>
<li>time frame (warning time, duration, time of day, week, year);</li>
<li>Manageability (whether anything can be done about it).</li>
</ul>
<p>For each hazard, these characteristics may mean different things. In a cyclone, for example, intensity might relate to wind speed, whereas in an earthquake, intensity relates to the number and strength of earth tremors. The example in the following section deals with flooding.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="description-of-a-flood-hazard">Description of a flood hazard<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#description-of-a-flood-hazard" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Flood intensity. Flood intensity may be described by height, class, depth, flow rate, and speed. Height. Flood height is often described in relation to a fixed marker, such as a post with heights marked on it, often placed in an arbitrary position near the river. Thus, a river height of 4 meters at one point on the river may be a fairly normal height, whereas the same height at a different place will indicate that the river is in flood.</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Class. Floods may also be described in terms of classes.</p>
<p>Definitions will vary from country to country, but the following are typical:</p>
<ul>
<li>Minor flooding  flooding that causes inconveniences, such as the closure of minor roads and the submergence of low-level bridges.</li>
<li>Moderate flooding  low-lying areas are inundated, requiring the removal of livestock and evacuation of some houses; main traffic bridges may also be submerged.</li>
<li>Major flooding  extensive rural areas are flooded, with properties and towns isolated; large urban areas are also flooded.</li>
</ul>
<p>In flood warnings, both the heights and the flood classes are often given for different points on a river; local people who receive the warnings may use their prior experience of either description to decide how to act.</p>
<p>Depth. Another way of describing the intensity of floods is to relate flood heights to the floor levels of buildings that may be affected. This gives an idea of depth and is very useful for planning evacuation, land use, and building protection.</p>
<p>Flow rate. Flow rate describes the volume of water flowing past a particular point in a given time period, and the sections are either cusecs (cubic feet per second).</p>
<p>This method of description is often used in relation to dam safety, as a very high flow rate over a dam with insufficient spillway capacity may lead to dam failure.</p>
<p>Speed. Flood intensity may also be described in terms of the speed of the water at a given point. This is a useful measure since speed, coupled with water depth, will indicate the scale of damage of which the moving water is capable.</p>
<p>Description of technological hazards</p>
<p>Technological hazards are caused by the processes and materials of life in an industrialized world.</p>
<p>They include:</p>
<p>a. The Transport of People and Materials (By Road, Rail, Air, or Sea);
b. The Use of Heavy or Fast-Moving Machinery;
c. The Use of High Pressure, High Temperature, Electricity, Etc.;</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>5 studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>d. The Manufacture, Storage, Use, and Disposal of Hazardous Materials.</p>
<p>The reasons for performing industrial hazard analysis depend on the perspective of those involved, and are summarized in the following paragraphs.</p>
<p>The community, including some members of the government, expects industry to be completely "safe". Attitudes towards the safety of industry tend to differ from attitudes towards safety in other activities, such as driving and sports, and are often unrealistic. Often, certain industries are targeted, while other less safe ones are ignored. For example, the degree of concern about industries involved in radioactive-related activities may be disproportionate compared with concern about other types of industries.</p>
<p>Industries are under increasing pressure from the community and government to minimize the risk of employee accidents and larger hazardous events that could affect the community. One measure that allows them to appear responsible is to perform hazard analysis.</p>
<p>Hazard analysis, coupled with comprehensive safety systems, has the potential to increase the viability of industry. Those industries that are aware of the principles of risk management may have differing degrees of concern about the risks involved in their activities, and may see sufficient benefit from the risks posed to allow them to continue at their current levels, or may consider the cost of reducing the risks to be too high. Industries that are unaware of the degree of risk involved in their activities or of the principles of risk management would probably resist any expenditure on hazard analysis or comprehensive safety systems.</p>
<p>A wide variation in the degree of safety of different types of industry, and in the interest that industrial management would have in performing hazard analysis, may therefore be expected.</p>
<p>Various government agencies may have the following involvement:</p>
<ul>
<li>ensuring that industry poses little threat to public safety from fire, explosion, or toxic emissions;</li>
<li>ensuring that workers' safety is within acceptable limits, with regard largely to minor accidents and injury and chronic toxic effects;</li>
<li>ensuring that public health is not affected adversely by chronic and acute toxic effects of industry;</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>ensuring that damage to the environment (including people) in the form of dust, smoke, noise, odors, gas, and liquid pollutants is minimized;</li>
<li>ensuring that land-use proposals (concerned with large areas rather than specific industries) involving hazardous industry zoning are appropriate;</li>
<li>Ensuring that emergency planning is appropriate for hazardous industry and surrounding areas.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="manageability">Manageability<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#manageability" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>The manageability of an industrial hazard for emergency preparedness purposes indicates what can be done about the hazard in terms of planning, training, and education, and carrying out drills. Regarding prevention, manageability of industrial hazards can be described in terms of the following types of controls, often called the "hierarchy of control":</p>
<ul>
<li>elimination (process or material);</li>
<li>replacement (process or material);</li>
<li>Reduction (quantity of material, pressure, temperature, etc.);</li>
<li>engineering control (over process or material);</li>
<li>separation from people, property, or environment;</li>
<li>administrative control;</li>
<li>emergency procedures;</li>
<li>Personal protection.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="vulnerability-assessment-hazard-analysis">Vulnerability assessment/Hazard Analysis<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#vulnerability-assessment-hazard-analysis" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h3 level="3" id="the-value-of-vulnerability-assessment">The value of vulnerability assessment<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-value-of-vulnerability-assessment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h3></div>
<p>Vulnerability assessment (also known as "hazard analysis", "threat assessment", and "risk assessment") is a procedure for identifying hazards and determining their possible effects on a community, activity, or organization.</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>8</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>It provides information essential for:</p>
<ul>
<li>Sustainable development (because development will be undermined without programs and strategies to reduce vulnerability);</li>
<li>Prevention, mitigation, and preparedness (without knowledge of what is likely to go wrong, and what the effects will be, it is impossible to be effectively prepared and difficult to prevent problems);</li>
<li>Emergency response (many emergencies seriously disrupt transportation and communications, and information may become either unreliable or non-existent; vulnerability assessment will suggest where most of the damage might occur);</li>
<li>Emergency recovery (vulnerability assessment can provide a baseline that describes the prior condition of the community, against which the effectiveness of recovery work can be compared).</li>
</ul>
<p>There are numerous ways of assessing vulnerability. The process described in this manual consists of a series of steps, each containing a number of techniques.</p>
<p>Some of these steps are hazard identification, community and environmental analysis, and hazard description. In turn, there are techniques for identifying hazards, for describing the people, property, and environment they may affect, and for describing hazards.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="c2319936">The Meaning of Vulnerability<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#c2319936" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Vulnerability is the result of a number of factors that increase the chance that a community will be unable to deal with an emergency. Not all sections of a community are vulnerable to hazards, but most are vulnerable to some degree.</p>
<p>Vulnerability comprises two aspects  susceptibility and resilience.</p>
<p><strong>Susceptibility</strong>: concerns the factors operating in a community that allow a hazard to cause an emergency; examples of such factors range from a communitys level of development to its location in an earthquake-prone area.</p>
<p><strong>Resilience</strong>: is the communitys ability to withstand the damage caused by emergencies and disasters; it is a function of the various factors that allow a community to respond to and recover from emergencies.</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="vulnerability-assessment">VULNERABILITY ASSESSMENT<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#vulnerability-assessment" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>Community and the individuals of which they are composed have a number of general needs during and after emergencies, of which personal safety is the most obvious. Thus, caring for the injured and protecting people from further harm are of paramount importance, together with providing food, drink, and shelter.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="the-meaning-of-hazard">The meaning of "hazard"<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#the-meaning-of-hazard" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>In emergency management, "hazard" has various definitions. This "hazard" is any phenomenon that has the potential to cause disruption or damage to people and their environment.</p>
<p>Other definitions within the context of emergency management include:</p>
<ul>
<li>A threat to people and what they value;</li>
<li>A threat to life, well-being, material goods, and environment from the extremes of natural processes and technology;</li>
<li>Potential for an agent or process to do harm.</li>
</ul>
<p>Common to all these definitions is the potential for harm.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="a-hazard-is-not-an-event">A hazard is not an event<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#a-hazard-is-not-an-event" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>It is the potential for an event. Thus "flood", in a general sense or even as applied to a particular place, is a hazard. An actual flood is an "incident", "emergency", or "disaster", depending on the damage it causes or how well it is managed.</li>
</ul>
<p>A vulnerability assessment relating to a flood in a particular community examines the potential for flood events and the possible effects of such events.</p>
<p>Analyzing a flood that has occurred is not a vulnerability assessment because each event is unique, and emergency management based on a specific event will contain too many unjustified assumptions. What is true of one flood will not necessarily be true of another.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id="communicating-in-an-emergency">COMMUNICATING IN AN EMERGENCY<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#communicating-in-an-emergency" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h1></div>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>8</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>At the completion of this topic, you should be able to:</p>
<ul>
<li>Indicate how day-to-day communication differs from communication during an incident.</li>
<li>Identify strategies for communicating effectively in an emergency situation.</li>
<li>Select the most appropriate form of communication for a given situation.</li>
<li>Indicate how social media and other communications technology can be used to communicate with members of the community.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="introduction-in-an-emergency">Introduction in an Emergency<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#introduction-in-an-emergency" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>During an incident, communication with the community becomes especially critical. Emergency communications may include alerts and warnings; directives about evacuation, curfews, and other self-protective actions; and information about response status, family members, available assistance, and other matters that impact response and recovery.</p>
<p>Well-conceived and effectively delivered emergency messages can help ensure public safety, protect property, facilitate response efforts, elicit cooperation, instill public confidence, and help families reunite.</p>
<p>The extent to which people respond to a warning message is influenced by many factors, including individual characteristics and perceptions, whether the message comes from a credible source, how the message is delivered, and the message itself.</p>
<p>Communication tools to choose include: in-person events, print and broadcast media, and Internet and social media. Each has advantages and limitations depending on ones communication objective and the intended audience.</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Whatever communication tools used, be sure your emergency communications are clear, contain specific and adequate information, are in sync with other information being disseminated, and are accessible to the whole community.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="delivering-effective-emergency-communications">Delivering Effective Emergency Communications<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#delivering-effective-emergency-communications" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>There are three aspects of communicating in an emergency:</p>
<ul>
<li>Emergency Communication Challenges</li>
<li>Emergency Communication Tools</li>
<li>Creating Effective Emergency Communications</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="emergency-communication-challenges">Emergency Communication Challenges<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#emergency-communication-challenges" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Public information during an incident important functions as follows:</p>
<ul>
<li>Save lives and reduce injury. Knowing the proper protective actions to take enables people to reduce their risk.</li>
<li>Protect property and the environment. Understanding how to mitigate risk to property and the environment may lessen the damage inflicted by disasters.</li>
<li>Facilitate the tactical response by calming fears and managing expectations. People who know what to expect are more likely to follow instructions and allow responders to do their jobs.</li>
<li>Educate, inform, and change behavior and attitudes. An educated public is more likely to prepare for emergencies and be ready when they occur.</li>
<li>Seek the public's cooperation. Whether the need is for volunteers to help with sandbagging, citizens to cooperate with investigators, or residents to evacuate their homes, public information is an instrument that can help make it happen.</li>
<li>Instill public confidence. Providing timely, accurate, and understandable information builds confidence in emergency management's competence.</li>
<li>Provide information to help families reunite. Public information about shelter message boards, hotlines, survivor registries, and other linkages can help reunite families and enable them to move forward with their recovery.</li>
</ul>
<p>Emergency communications differ from routine communications in several ways:</p>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<p>8</p>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>Barriers: It is more difficult for people to hear messages during an emergency. Stress, change of routine, and lack of sleep all can be hurdles to overcome when communicating during emergencies.</p>
<p>Timeliness: If official answers are not available, rumor and speculation quickly fill the information vacuum. Then, not only must you disseminate correct information, but you also need to counter any misinformation that circulated. To use media in a timely fashion, learn local media news cycles and deadlines.</p>
<p>For example, if a news event occurs at 4:00 p.m., you can most likely get it on the radio immediately, on television in time for the evening report, and into the next mornings edition of the local paper. Online news outlets can be updated at any time.</p>
<p>Required Response: Emergency warnings differ from other kinds of messages because their purpose is to elicit a specific response from the public, rather than merely raise awareness or provide knowledge.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="factors-that-affect-response">Factors That Affect Response.<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#factors-that-affect-response" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Successful warnings are those that are taken seriously and responded to in a timely and effective manner. Multiple factors may have an impact on whether people respond to a warning, including: Individual characteristicsAge, education, language, access and functional needs, family composition, and length of residency, among others.</p>
<p>PerceptionsPrevious experience with a hazard and perceptions of proximity and risk.</p>
<p>Message sourceWho issues the warning, credibility of the warning source, and the level of trust in that source.</p>
<p>The message itselfAccuracy, clarity, timeliness, consistency, and specificity of the message; and its focus on immediate needs.</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="community-factors">Community Factors<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#community-factors" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Type of community: Residents of rural communities may have more difficulty receiving warnings than those living in urban areas.</li>
<li>Level of community interaction: People who have more contacts in the community will receive more warnings and are more likely to act; also, they are more likely to trust officials.</li>
<li>Family composition: Families, more than individuals, tend to heed evacuation warnings. Research indicates that people tend to confer with family, extended family, and friends prior to</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<p>making a decision. They do this to ensure that their loved ones are safe and also to determine whether they may need to provide protection for their loved ones.</p>
<p>Their decisions are based on the following factors related to family composition:</p>
<ul>
<li>Family network: People are more likely to act if they have relatives nearby who may warn them and offer them short-term shelter.</li>
<li>Presence of children: Concern for childrens safety will elicit quicker response from parents.</li>
<li>Presence of pets: People often view their pets as they would their children and will take action to protect them. However, whereas families with children usually act more quickly to take precautions, in emergencies requiring evacuation, people with pets may endanger their own lives by refusing to evacuate, because many public shelters do not allow pets.</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="experiential-factors">Experiential Factors<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#experiential-factors" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Interpretation of message: When different people listen to the same message, there may be a variation in what they hear, leading to different interpretation and response.</li>
<li>Previous experiences: Often people will rely on their previous experiences with the hazard to determine what actions they initially take (or dont take).</li>
<li>Observations: Individual responses to warnings vary, but most people will seek some form of confirmation. For example, some people will look for more information through environmental cues, while others will seek to contact other trusted sources. Optimism bias (thinking that disasters happen to other people) is overcome with confirmation.</li>
<li>Perception of risk/proximity: People tend to make a rapid assessment of the relative safety of their location, producing an emergent perception of risk. If their perception of personal risk is high, people will act quickly. When the perception is low, they will delay acting</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="individual-factors">Individual Factors<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#individual-factors" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<ul>
<li>Age: Children and older adults may not be able to receive and/or respond appropriately to alerts and warnings. Many in this group may also need assistance.</li>
</ul>
<p>This document is available on</p>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h1 level="1" id=""><span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"></span></h1></div>
<p>studocu</p>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
<ul>
<li>
<p>Language: Non-English-speaking persons may not understand warnings that are provided in English. Communities with high percentages of non-English-speaking people should issue warnings in the primary language(s) of the population as well as in English.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Length of residency: Transients, tourists, and newcomers to the area lack knowledge of local hazards and the history of local disasters, so they may react differently.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Access and functional needs: Individuals with access and functional needs may need alerts in accessible formats and additional time and assistance for evacuating. Accessibility of alert and warning messages refers to whether individuals hear and understand them. Alternative alert and warning methods are needed for individuals with access and functional needs such as those who are blind or deaf or have low vision or hearing. Both audio and equivalent text messages should be available.</p>
</li>
<li>
<p>Level of individual preparedness: People who have taken the time to prepare for hazards (i.e., they have a plan and disaster supply kit, and have exercised the plan) are more likely to heed warnings and act appropriately. Getting the preparedness buy-in is often the challenge.</p>
</li>
</ul>
<div data-testid="stHeadingWithActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-1p9ibxm e1icttdg2"><h2 level="2" id="emergency-communication-tools">Emergency Communication Tools<span data-testid="stHeaderActionElements" class="st-emotion-cache-gi0tri e1icttdg3"><a href="#emergency-communication-tools" class="st-emotion-cache-yinll1 e1icttdg1"><svg xmlns="http://www.w3.org/2000/svg" width="16" height="16" viewBox="0 0 24 24" fill="none" stroke="currentColor" stroke-width="2" stroke-linecap="round" stroke-linejoin="round"><path d="M15 7h3a5 5 0 0 1 5 5 5 5 0 0 1-5 5h-3m-6 0H6a5 5 0 0 1-5-5 5 5 0 0 1 5-5h3"></path><line x1="8" y1="12" x2="16" y2="12"></line></svg></a></span></h2></div>
<p>Choosing the right communication tool is a matter of getting the right information to the right people at the right time so they can make the right decisions. Remember, the most effective communication tool is one that:</p>
<ul>
<li>Reaches the target audience.</li>
<li>Gets information to the audience when they need it, for as long as they need it.</li>
<li>Can be expected to deliver the message reliably.</li>
<li>Enhances comprehension of the message content.</li>
<li>Can be accessed within resource limitations</li>
</ul>
<p>Tools and methods for emergency communications include:</p>
<ul>
<li>In-person eventsbriefings and public meetings.</li>
<li>Print medianewspapers and magazines.</li>
<li>Broadcast mediatelevision and radio.</li>
<li>Internet and social media.</li>
</ul>
<p>Downloaded by Patrick Ngobiro (<a href="mailto:kenyaewakili@gmail.com" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">kenyaewakili@gmail.com</a>)</p>
